4.1. Mechanism of MO-to-MOF conversion

MO-to-MOF conversion rates are subjected to MO composition, morphology (shape and size), surface roughness and pH-dependent surface charge.257–259 Tuning these MO features enables fabrication of materials with desirable MOF structures, without the need for surface functionalizations like self-assembled monolayers (SAMs).260,261 For example, it was found that the formation of Cu3(BTC)2 from CuO sheets is easily feasible, whereas no HKUST-1 was obtained when CuO microcrystals were used under the same conditions.258 Moreover, choosing suitable solvent(s) in solution-based syntheses is as important for the final MOF morphology as the selection of appropriate MO nanoparticle shape and size. This is exemplified for ZnO, with mixtures of octanol and 2-methylimidazole (2-mim or Hmim) not resulting in ZIF-8 formation, whilst ethanol addition amplified MO dissolution and allowed MOF growth.262 To fully use the advantages of MOs, a good understanding of the reaction kinetics as a crucial variable of MO-to-MOF conversions is important. Hence, to enable targeted and controllable synthesis relations among competing rates are the key for which the following mechanism dominates: dissolution-crystallization or template-assisted transition, where the latter results in a controlled MO-to-MOF conversion (Fig. 17).
Fig. 17 Graphic representation of IBUs (blue) and OBUs (orange) during different competing rates: MO dissolution, diffusion, heterogeneous and homogeneous MOF nucleation; resulting in MO-to-MOF full conversion (shape preservation), core–shell structure (diffusion limitations) and diverse single-crystals (dissolution-crystallization).

The rates that govern MO-to-MOF conversion are MO dissolution, metallic cations/ligands diffusion, MOF nucleation, and MOF growth. High MO dissolution rates paired with sufficient diffusion can result in random crystallization within the solution associated with local supersaturations (homogeneous nucleation).259 In this case, particles grow in the solution without the templating effect, working similarly to soluble salts but without accompanying acidic by-products. On the other hand, when the MO dissolution is balanced in a way that MOF nucleation can occur locally around the substrate (heterogeneous nucleation) without too high diffusion rates, the MO can serve as a self-sacrificial template. This shape preserving mechanism can allow the full transformation of the MO substrate into MOF and hence the perpetuation of the original morphology. The progressive metallic cation-release as a result of the MO dissolution causes a concentration gradient of IBUs from the substrate to the solution, allowing the MOF to maintain on the MO and eventually to entirely replace it. However, to obtain full conversion, rates have to be matched perfectly and most often the results of MO-to-MOF reactions are core–shell-like structures explainable by diffusion limitations as a result of a closed MOF shell around the substrates before total transformation. Accordingly, to achieve complete conversion or a desired core–shell structure respectively, for each reaction an understanding of the effect of the different reaction parameters on the system as well as the choice of a suitable MO morphology is required.

It is important to point out that the physicochemical properties of MOFs depend on their nanostructure, which is not observed for bulk materials.244,263 Thus, designing the right route for MOF assembly is a matter of relevance for scaffolding features and application. Reaction conditions and their impact on kinetics vary depending on the chosen method. Regardless, there is a general aspect for so-called structured-directing agents (SDA) including guest molecules, mineralizers, surfactants and swelling agents.264 Whereas solvent-free reactions for MOF self-assembly are feasible, it is challenging to fabricate uniform and distinguished MOF nanomaterials that way. For most commonly used solvent-based syntheses the main concern is the choice of solvent(s), solvent ratio, precursor concentration ratio and reaction times. All of these factors can be used effectively if the appropriate temperature is applied during the processes, as well as enhancements such as radiation, sonication or electric field.

Up to now only a couple of techniques have been proposed for MO-to-MOF preparation, in which MO takes part precisely as a self-sacrificial template and metal source. It has to be differentiated from MO utilization for MOF assembly, where the role of MO is only being a non-self-sacrificial substructure for MOF assembly, i.e. inactive MO is passively responsible for surface mounted MOF morphology. However metallic cations are not released from the solid minerals and they do not take part in MOF formation directly. A representative example of such a procedure is the heterostructure of Fe3O4 covered with ZIF-8. The iron oxide precursor was injected in the oil phase as a microreactor, with the MO core being formed in the first step, on top of which in the next step a MOF shell was created, resulting in Fe3O4@ZIF-8 composite structures.265 Another example is introduced by Zhao et al.266 They coated different polymer fibres via the atomic layer deposition (ALD) technique with a thin layer of Al2O3, ZnO, or TiO2 for nucleation of high-quality HKUST-1 crystals uniformly on top of the fibre surface coatings. The MOF was prepared in a solvothermal reaction in a solution of metal salt precursors and the respective organic linker, where MO was not used as a self-sacrificial layer. It was shown that the MOF crystal size depends on the coating used, increasing from ZnO over Al2O3 to TiO2. This is explained through a difference in wettability, surface roughness, and isoelectric point of the different ALD coatings. The thus achieved different ‘MOF-PP/ALD’-fibres were then investigated for adsorption of hazardous gases like NH3 or H2S.

In the following we discuss reports in which only MO was used as a self-sacrificial template and metal source for the MOF formation.

4.2. ZnO as a metal precursor

One of the first successful MO-to-MOF conversion approaches was reported by Zou et al.267 A zinc wafer was activated by H2O2 to form surface-hydroxide layers, on which a MOF film was subsequently deposited by a hydrothermal reaction. Although this approach was not a direct application of MO, it allowed avoiding the use of salts as a metal source and thus it eliminated the problems associated with acidic anions. In the end, the formation of Zn3(BTC)2 films exhibited spike-like uniformity with large scale orders. In the same year Friščić et al.268 used specifically MOs as precursors due to their low-cost and availability. In the single-step neutralization of MOs with dicarboxylic acids the MOF structure was formed, with water as a sole by-product. Their approach based on ZnO and fumaric acid in a liquid-assisted grinding (LAG) reaction led to the formation of materials varied in morphology by using different grinding liquids, i.e. ethanol (3D MOF), mixture of ethanol and water with molar ratio 1:1 (2D MOF) and various amounts of water (1D zigzag or linear polymers). Interestingly, Lin et al.269 presented the formation of ZIF-8 (in their article described as MAF-4) by solvent-free grinding of ZnO or Zn(OH)2. At room temperature, substrates did not react; however, after heating at 100 °C for 24 h ZIF-8 was obtained. It was possible to shorten the reaction time to 12 h by heating the mixture to 180 °C. Powder X-ray diffraction indicated that increasing the temperature resulted in sharper and weaker reflexes, which are typical for the guest-free ZIF-8 structure. This method allowed fabrication of high purity ZIF with 100% yield, specific surface area of 1450 m2 g−1 and with relatively homogeneous sub-micrometre crystals. In further reports MOFs with higher surface area such as Zn(mim)2 (1961 m2 g−1) and new Co(mim)2 (2070 m2 g−1) were also prepared.270 Zn and Co imidazoles were obtained in a direct acid–base reaction between ZnO/CoO/Co(OH)2. Synthesized ZIF network topologies revealed dependence on the presence of small amounts of co-solvents such as 1-butanol, pyridine or 4-methylpyridine, as well as on the heating rate. Addition of a third species can lower the topological density leading to open-framework compounds. In the case of temperature, the increase enhances ligand deprotonation, which directly leads to homoleptic complex formation. Heating rate, however, if kept slow provides higher crystallinity of Zn- and Co-derived MOFs, as well as larger size of crystallites.

Stassen et al.260 also focused on ZnO with the aim of the transformation of the resulting two- and 3D MO patterns to the ZIF-8 pattern by a convenient two-step procedure via sputtering or electrochemical growth, respectively. It was conducted through a green solvent-free reaction of ZnO precursor films and patterns with a melted organic linker (Hmim). Variation of film thickness and density allowed the control of the ZIF-8 crystal size due to the change of Zn2+ supply. In a follow up work uniform ZnO thin films of precisely controlled thickness were deposited using ALD technology, both on a flat substrate and high-aspect-ratio features, which then have been transformed into ZIF-8 using chemical vapor deposition (CVD), providing the linker as a vapor instead of a solution.271 Different from the CVD method, solvothermal transformations need precise matching of MO dissolution and MOF crystallization rates, as slight local differences in dissolution rate, coupled with fast diffusion of the released metal ions can lead to the formation of rough films with many defects. Cross-sectional analysis via TEM allowed the study of the MO-to-MOF conversion efficiency: complete transformation could be observed for precursor films of less than 10 nm thickness. Thicker films do not show full transformation because of the diffusion limitations of the organic linker to the oxide and/or of zinc to the growing crystal interface. However, the expansion of around 10 nm film thickness is expected due to the fabrication of highly porous ZIF-8 from the dense ZnO. The thickness increase indicated linkage accordingly to the theoretical value based on the ratio of the crystallographic volume occupied per mol of zinc in MO and MOF.

To show the general applicability of the MOF-CVD approach, ZIF-61 (imidazole), ZIF-72 (4,5-dichloroimidazole), and the Co-based ZIF-67 (using CoO films and Hmim) were deposited under similar experimental conditions, as well as Cu(II)-dicarboxylates using copper hydroxide films as precursor films and fumaric acid and BDC as organic linkers.272 A different approach for obtaining ZIF-8 membranes was suggested by Li et al.273 from Zn(OH)2 nanostrands. It is liquid-based synthesis in which hydroxide and Hmim are left in ethanol and water solution (1:4 by volume) at room temperature for 24 h. To achieve well-defined and intergrown morphology sodium formate was added. It resulted in a uniform layer of rhombic ZIF crystals. Increasing the alcohol volume in the water:ethanol ratio led to smaller and more loosely packed ZIF-8 crystals. However, using mainly water as a solvent caused formation of more free-standing crystals in the solution, instead on the surface.274,275 Out of 900 nm thick precursors 800 nm of ZIF-8 was formed. In the case of membrane syntheses often diffusion limitation occurs, thus the reaction continues to the point of achieving compact structures, which will impede metallic cations and organic linker diffusion.

Zhan et al.276 used ZnO rods to build semiconductor@MOF core–shell heterostructures via a self-template synthetic strategy in a solvothermal reaction, whereby the MO templates themselves provided metal ions initiating ZIF-8 growth without the requirement of any surface modifications. The growth process of the ZIF-shell involved firstly growth of ZIF-8 nanoparticles on the MO's surface, which then formed a continuous layer during prolonged reaction time. Similar to Stassen et al.271 a saturation of ZIF-8 growth due to diffusion limitations could be observed. The ZIF-8 shell was found to be very compact without any pinholes and no free-standing ZIF-8 nanoparticles were found at any reaction time under optimized reaction conditions. Changing of the reaction temperature and solvent composition led to huge variations in the quality of the MOF-shell and amount of free-standing ZIF-8 nanoparticles, as the balance between dissolution and coordination rate was disturbed. It has to be noted that a solvent mixture of DMF and water is crucial because Hmim works both as an etching agent to dissolve ZnO providing Zn2+ ions and as a ligand for the ZIF-8 formation. In pure DMF the etching ability of the ligand is too weakened for ZIF-8 formation to occur, whereas in pure water the dissolution rate dominates, and thus free-standing nanoparticles of dense MOFs are the dominant species.

Khaletskaya et al.261 used ALD and magnetron sputtering for localized deposition of MO thin films, which were then converted into thin MOF membranes. The resulting thin films were analysed in cross-sectional view (prepared by focused ion beam [FIB] milling) using TEM, scanning transmission electron microscopy-electron energy loss spectroscopy (STEM-EELS) and energy-dispersive X-ray elemental mapping (STEM-EDX). The film fabrication approach was then extended to amorphous Al2O3 layers pre-deposited by ALD, which were converted into a dense homogeneous [Al(OH)(1,4-NDC)]n film. ZnO particles (as well as functionalized ZnO particles) can also be used as heterogeneous nucleation agents for the rapid growth of a number of different MOFs, such as MOF-5 (terephthalic acid), IRMOF-3 (2-aminoterephthalic acid), IRMOF-8 (2,6-naphthalenedicarboxylic acid) and IRMOF-10 (4,4′-biphenyl dicarboxylic acid) (Fig. 18). In a solution of dissolved metal salt and organic linker, ZnO particles of varying size (micro- and nanoparticles) were immersed leading to different MOF types.277


Fig. 18 SEM images of MOFs prepared with different ligands for 1 h at 95 °C. In the insets: the corresponding ligand structure. From left to right: MOF-5, IRMOF-3, MOF-4, IRMOF-8, and IRMOF-10 (Reprinted with permission.277 Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society).

It was shown that the use of nano-sized nucleation agents allowed a decrease in nucleation time, as well as the temperature required for the MOF synthesis.277 Further, it was demonstrated that the ZnO seed-induced rapid MOF formation is not simply driven by an increase in the pH (which facilitates ligand deprotonation) of the bulk reaction solution. In fact, increasing the number of seeds resulted in faster nucleation and an increased number of MOF crystals but decreased in crystal size were observed. Using paper support and immersing one side in a solution of ZnO nanoparticles and the other side in a solution of dissolved MOF precursors paired with a lateral flow of the solutions induced by capillary forces led to MOF nucleation at the contact interface. Further, ZnO seeds were used to precisely control the position of MOF crystal formation within microchannels (Fig. 19).


Fig. 19 Outline of the heterogeneous nucleation of MOFs by pure and functionalized ZnO microparticles (a, c and d) and nanoparticles (b, e, f and g) demonstrated in this work. ZnO nanoparticles can also be used to induce the growth of MOF crystals on paper and within microfluidic channels as shown in (f and g), respectively (Reprinted with permission.277 Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society).

Meckler et al.278 used the ZnO-to-ZIF conversion to fabricate a layered composite of sub-micron-thick ZIF-7 and ZIF-8 films on polymers of intrinsic microporosity (PIMs). It was found that parameters like temperature, ligand concentration and reaction time failed to give control over film growth and quality; however, changes in solvent composition enabled significant control. Reaction mixtures of DMF-to-water with up to 50 vol% water yielded relatively smooth and continuous ZIF-8 films, suggesting the formation of many ZIF-8 nuclei and comparatively slow crystal growth. At water content beyond 50 vol%, significantly less ZnO was visible in cross-sectional SEM images and the ZIF-8 grain size increased, resulting in faceted, isolated crystals adhered to the polymer surface instead of a continuous film. Uninterrupted, nearly pure ZIF-8 coatings could be obtained from 15 nm thick ZnO films with 50 vol% water reaction mixtures. Variant preparation of substructures for ZIF-8 was suggested by Al-Kutubi et al.279 They synthesized ZIF-8 thin films on top of an electrochemically grown ZnO rod array. They were fabricated through the deposition of the linker on top of the rod array, with subsequent solvent evaporation to form a thin uniform linker film. Heating the sample in an oven resulted in the formation of a ZIF-8 film on the ZnO arrays. Liu et al.280 prepared a ZIF-8-ZnAl-NO3 layered double hydroxide (LDH) composite membrane by partial conversion of the ZnAl-NO3 LDH membrane in a one-step reaction for enhanced H2/CH4 separation. Zhang and coworkers281 converted ZnO to ZIF-8 as precursors/sacrificial templates to construct Zn-based nanoarchitecture electrode materials for high energy storage lithium-ion batteries. ZIF-8 was thereby transformed into an amorphous carbon framework and ZnO quantum dots in a thermal decomposition process. Del Rio et al.282 used a sacrificial ZnO coating which is transformed into ZIF-8 to create polystyrene-ZnO-ZIF-8 core-double-shell particles with an intermediate shell of residual ZnO. Increasing the reaction time and temperature did not show any influence on ZnO conversion into ZIF-8, suggesting interrupted growth due to the diffusion limitations after a certain point. Maserati and coworkers252 studied the formation of M-MOF-74 series (M2(dobpdc), M = Mg, Mn, Co, Ni, Zn) using different bivalent MOs as precursors in a solvothermal reaction, following a dissolution-crystallization mechanism, with the MO dissolution being the rate-limiting factor.258 The use of MOs as precursors allowed them to reduce reaction times by several orders of magnitude, achieving MOF formation in a matter of minutes.

Sun et al.283 reported the formation of Zn-derived MOF shells: ZIF-8 and ZIF-71 on ZnO particles as cores, reacting with Hmim and 4,5-dichloroimidazole, respectively. To prevent aggregation of minerals their surfaces were modified with PVP, which additionally promoted retention of the released metallic cations near the template. Three types of colloidal ZnO particles with different sizes were fabricated: 95 nm, 230 nm and 320 nm. Synthesized MOF product from 95 nm ZnO showed a rhombic–dodecahedral shape with 270 nm diameter. TEM and TGA revealed that ZnO remains inside the structure, with 12.8 wt% of non-converted oxide. It was caused by the small pore apertures present in ZIF, resulting in the above-mentioned Zn2+ diffusion limitations. The sizes of ZnO@ZIF core–shell particles were dependent on the template size, diffusion and nucleation/growth rates, which were mainly influenced by the ratio of water/DMF. Water enhances ZnO dissolution, whereas DMF reduces it. Both, however, if used separately cause heterogeneous nucleation of MOF nanoislands on the mineral core. The solvent effect was investigated manifesting hybrid core–shell particles in aqueous solution. Changing the thickness of the ZIF-8 shell was possible with DMF addition. Treatment of reactants with only DMF led to the fabrication of partially enshrouded structures, with one or several MOF domains on the mineral template. Furthermore, limited Zn2+ diffusion slower than nucleation and growth ended up in surrounding the core with a thin ZIF layer and thereby further thickening of the shell was terminated. Size-dependent ZIF formation is linked to the nucleation energy requirements. It was suggested that lower energy is needed for the growth of ZIF-8 on the oxide surface than its nucleation. Thus smaller ZnO particles form fewer nucleation sites, ending up with more homogeneous shells. In consequence, if the ZnO precursor particle is larger, longer distances have to be overcome between nucleated MOF crystallites and Zn2+ from MOF-free sites. Therefore, it is preferable to form more nuclei on the larger core, leading to intergrown crystal domains. Successful approach of combining MOF from MO with different types of material was introduced by Mazaj et al.284 They hybridized MOF-5 and HKUST-1 with polyHIPE by using ZnO and CuO as precursors, respectively. MO nanoparticles embedded into polymer walls formed a nanocomposite, which was subjected to organic ligand solution under solvothermal conditions. TGA and XRD measurements confirmed nearly entire MO-to-MOF conversion into cubic MOF-5 and octahedral HKUST-1. Applying porous polymers without acidic anions stabilized final materials, by avoiding direct exposure of the formed MOF crystals to monomeric solution.

There are several proposals of a mechanochemical top-down approach for ZIF-8 synthesis from ZnO grinding with Hmim. Reduction or total absence of solvents unlocked novel prospects for prominent composites with MOF. Yue et al.285 synthesized both ZIF-8 nanoparticles and ZIF-8 thin films from the corresponding ZnO nanoscopic precursors through solid-state reactions without the involvement of conventional dissolution processes. According to the authors, this transformation process can be understood as an anion-exchange process, involving the displacement of the lattice oxide anions with imidazolate anions. The dependence of the phase purity on the molar ratios of Hmim to ZnO was shown, e.g. producing phase pure ZIF-8 nanoparticles using a molar ratio of 5:2 (Hmim to ZnO).286 Building upon this work, Yue et al.287 prepared a multi-walled carbon nanotube/zeolitic ZIF-8 composite by a template free ‘brick and mortar’ method: ZnO nanoparticles were adsorbed onto the surface of the tubes and then reacted with the organic linker. Strong interactions between the linker and carbon surfaces led to the nucleation and growth of the ZIF-8 crystal solely on the tube's surface. ZnO precursor nanoparticles with an average size of 35 nm and no ZnO signal was observed in XRD measurements after the reaction. Involving new materials in milling during the MO-to-MOF transition was suggested by Li et al.288 in 2019. They introduced mechanochemistry-assisted solvent-free synthesis involving incorporation of Pd nanoparticles into the MOF structure for catalytic purposes. Using MO/hydroxide without solvents is highly preferable for bulk-synthetic industrial routes. Although it seems efficient it is often difficult to synthesize MOF nanomaterials distinguished in morphology via top-down strategies.

4.3. Al2O3 as a metal precursor

Besides ZnO another often applied MO for MOFs as a substructure and a source of metallic cations is alumina (Al2O3). Reboul et al.289 used Al2O3 in the form of a 2D honeycomb pattern and a 3D inverse opal structure as a metal reservoir and mesoscale SDA for construction of a mesoscopic MOF architecture (namely, coordination replication). [Al(OH)(1,4-NDC)]n was formed in a microwave-assisted reaction in an aqueous solution of the organic linker; MIL-100(Al) in a solution of H3BTC; MIL-53(Al) in a solution of H2BDC. In 2013 Mao et al.290 presented synthesis of oriented 3D MIL-110 rod arrays on a porous anodic aluminum oxide membrane (AAO) in the company of the hetero-metal hydroxide strands, which in this case were made of Cu(OH)2. The hexagonal column structure of the MOF was maintained when the Cu(OH)2 strand layer was utilized; the absence of such a layer led to only sheet-like products. Inductive coupled plasma emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) indicated remaining copper ions in solution after the procedure; thus, in the end pure MIL-110 was formed. Also Cd(OH)2 and Zn(OH)2 were tested as SDAs with the successful outcome. Nevertheless, the density of the MIL-110 rod arrays was highly dependent on the kinds of metal hydroxides used. Most dense were rods after Cu(OH)2 application, less dense after Cd(OH)2 and the least after Zn(OH)2. Another morphology impactful parameter was the time of the reaction. Proceeding the synthesis for 3 h led to irregular particle layers with Cu. Continuing the reaction to about 6–9 h resulted in dissolving copper-related structures, whilst thick and straight MIL-110 fibres with shrubbery-like morphology were obtained. After 24 h well-oriented rod arrays of the MOF were formed. Selecting an appropriate solvent was equally important for the material shape and size. For example, using octanol led to formation of sheet-like Cu-BTC belts, whereas a mixture of DMF and ethanol ended up in small, loosely packed HKUST-1 crystals. Thus, it was concluded that using only water promoted MIL-110 nucleation and further kept the material stable without the formation of different MOF impurities, in spite of the presence of other cations than Al3+ (e.g. Cu2+).291

Nakahama et al.292 introduced alumina as a metal source and SDA for fibrous macroscopic architectures of Al(OH)(L), where L is 1,4-naphthalene dicarboxylate (NDC) or BDC. The synthesis was performed in aqueous solution at 180 °C and for only 1 min because of the acceleration effect of microwave irradiation. Morphology control was realized by changing the pH of the mixture. Low pH 2.1 highly increased dissolution of the mineral, resulting in small fragmented MOF fibres. At pH 4.0, needle-like ‘rose thorns’ crystals were formed. Slightly higher pH 4.3 led to thickening of needles to a fibrous morphology, whereas rectangular crystals of fibres formed at pH 5.4. Fabricated Al-derived MOF structures were thicker than the Al2O3 template due to the lower density and thus bigger volume. Furthermore, the structural deformation phenomenon was noticed when the reaction was continued for 72 h at 400 °C. Block-type crystals of Al(OH)(BDC) transformed from closed-form to open-form, which is usually observed only for bulk crystals.293

Bechelany et al.294 made use of the so-called ‘reactive seeding’ approach, by converting a MO thin film (ZnO/Al2O3, deposited uniformly on polymer fibres using ALD) to a MOF by a solvothermal reaction (using both conventional and microwave-assisted heating) with a simple solution of the organic linker with different concentrations. ZnO coatings were converted into ZIF-8 in a methanolic solution of Hmim with conversion rates of up to 86% for microwave-assisted heating and up to 96% using conventional heating; Al2O3 coatings were converted into MIL-53-NH2 in an aqueous solution of 2-aminoterephthalic acid with conversion rates of up to 66% for microwave-assisted heating and up to 94% using conventional heating. Microwave-assisted reactions did not exhibit any influence on the crystal morphology or MO conversion regardless of Hmim concentration or the reaction time. Slight influence occurred when different 2-aminoterephthalic acid concentrations were used. Reactions using conventional heating led to non-uniform crystal growth and fibre coverage and showed a high dependence on both, linker concentration and reaction time, which resulted in the increased crystal size and conversion rate.

Li et al.295 utilized three different aluminium compounds, Al2O3, Al(OH)3 and boehmite, as stable metal sources for MIL-53(Al) formation via hydrothermal synthesis. In comparison to the traditional synthesis with aluminium salt, mineral sources proved sufficiency with higher yields, even if the molar ratio of metal source/organic linkers was lower. Additionally, very common pH decrease caused by proton release from H2BDC ligands and acidic anions from salt was eschewed. Furthermore, pH remained constant or even slightly basic due to the acid-based neutralization. Each substructure varied in shape and size, thus MOF morphology was different. After the synthesis, a ball-like Al(OH)3 cluster with a size above 100 μm transformed into MIL-53(Al) whose size was less than 5 μm. A lamellar sheet of boehmite was converted to a rod-like MOF. At the early stage of conversion, Al2O3 blocks were covered with a MOF structure, but after 72 h when the source material depleted, only crystal rods of MIL-53(Al) were noticeable in SEM images. Interestingly alumina-derived MOF indicated the highest specific surface area compared to the other substructures. Thus, it could be potentially a better candidate for industrial synthesis when porosity and yield are considered.

4.4. Cu-based sources

An unconventional proposition of preparation of the most known copper based MOF, HKUST-1, was presented by Guo et al.296 They prepared an HKUST-1 membrane out of Cu nets (thread diameter and void size were both 20 μm). The primarily metallic net was crystallized from Cu(NO3)2, and then oxidized to CuO before hydrothermal synthesis. It was found that the use of CuO was crucial for the continuous Cu2+ dissociation and homogeneous HKUST-1 crystallization. The SEM image indicated arranging of a defect-free structure of octahedral crystals (5–10 μm) on the net, which formed a 60 μm thick membrane. It led to the conclusion that a transfer of MO-to-MOF preserving morphology is also possible in the case of copper-derivatives.296,297 Majano and Pérerez-Ramírez298 synthesized HKUST-1 through a room temperature procedure in 5 min. The conversion rate of the hydroxide after such a short period of time was 82%, whereas full conversion took place after 5 h. Both MOF crystal size and growth were dependent on the initial Cu(OH)2 crystallite size (800 nm–1 μm) for both reaction times. However, morphology changed completely from layered elongated Cu(OH)2 crystals to the MOF octahedra. It is worth mentioning that the whole procedure was carried out in an aqueous–ethanolic solution, without DMF which impedes crystallization kinetics. Okada et al.299 presented HKUST-1 synthesis from Cu(OH)2 tubes. The high rate of the above-mentioned processes aggravates control of Cu3(BTC)2 size. After 1 s MOF with <200 nm average dimension was formed. At 25 s the first octahedral crystals appeared. After 1 min hydroxide tubes became shorter and Cu3(BTC)2 crystals became larger in size with a rougher surface. At 300 s crystals with 200–400 nm diameter were detectable which kept growing for a total of 1200 s (Fig. 20). After that no changes were observed.
Fig. 20 Evolution of the Cu(OH)2 nanotubes from the original shape investigated using SEM. (a) The substrate decorated with Cu(OH)2 nanotubes, once immersed in a 1,3,5-benzenetricarboxylic acid and alcoholic mixture, shows rapid conversion into a crystalline material. (b) The change can be detected within 1 s. (c–h) Further evolution leading towards the formation of a flat layer of MOF crystals. (i–l) Schematic representing the key steps in the morphological change during the Cu3(BTC)2 crystal formation using Cu(OH)2 nanotubes. (i and j) Change dominated by the pseudomorphic replication. (j and k) Crystal growth without the conservation of the initial tubular shape. (k and l) Change dominated by the Ostwald ripening mechanism (Reprinted with permission.299 Copyright 2014, John Wiley & Sons).

Another preparation of well-established size of Cu3(BTC)2 was presented by Takahashi's group. MOF crystals were prepared by conversion of Cu(OH)2 belts previously coated on a gold electrode.300 The ratio of ethanol:water has a high impact on Cu3(BTC)2 crystal size due to co-solvent polarity (dielectric constant), which affects ligand deprotonation. Higher hydroxide conversion of MOFs and larger octahedral crystals from several hundreds to more than 3 μm were obtained in 2:5 ratio (ethanol:water). In a more ethanolic mixture (5:2 ethanol:water) smaller crystals were formed. This observation implies that crystallization is dominated specifically by Ostwald ripening. More polar solvents like water (dielectric constant – 80.1) increase the acidity of a mixture due to the enhanced proton release from organic linkers. Less polar ethanol (dielectric constant – 24.5) limits deprotonation, which further leads to a decrease in Cu(OH)2 dissolution rate and thus a shorter diameter of Cu3(BTC)2.

In 2017 Falcaro et al.301 prepared centimetre-scale, aligned 2D and 3D MOF films directly from a Cu(OH)2 substrate. Following a heteroepitaxial growth mechanism, the synthesis was facilitated by precise matching of the structure metrics of the organic linker and the lattice parameters of the metal hydroxide substrate. MOF crystals being oriented in one direction, the out-of-plane one, have been previously synthesized using liquid phase epitaxy; however, MOF crystals being orientated in all three directions have not been demonstrated. Orientation along all three crystallographic axes could be achieved by conversion of a crystalline metal hydroxide surface.

Zhan and Zeng302 proposed applying Cu2O as a metal source for HKUST-1 formation in a solution based synthesis with co-solvents of DMF/ethanol, which indicated a nanoscale ‘domain growth’ mechanism. After MO dissolution the starting solution became heterogeneous in metallic cations; however, the presence of oxygen in the medium allowed converting the oxidation state of CuI to CuIIvia the following reaction: 2Cu2O + O2 + 8H+ → 4Cu2+ + 4H2O. Oxidized copper ions were able to react with the organic linker to form crystallites, which in time aggregated, producing larger MOF domains. Using Cu2O cubes with uniform size and shape (average edge size of 60 nm) led to the formation of 3D networks of nanocrystal MOF domains (∼20 nm). It was stated that for successful MO conversion into MOF networks it is necessary to provide a specific concentration of PVP (e.g. 0.48 g L−1) as a structure-directing agent. Also the polar co-solvents affected organic ligand dissociation and oxidative dissolution of ions from minerals. Under the optimized reaction temperature and time, it was possible to generate HKUST-1 with hierarchical porosity. Further investigation on the shape and size control demonstrated that ratios of MO/organic linker and solvent/co-solvent are crucial for the crystallization process and thus morphology of the final product. It was found that the required ratio of Cu2O/H3BTC and water/ethanol for fibres was 1:76 and 1:0.43, respectively; for rod-like crystals it was 1:38 and 1:1.2, respectively. A further decrease of organic ligand amount to 1:19 ratio led to the growth of thin nanosheets with a rectangular shape.303

4.5. Other MOs as metal reservoirs

The concept of MO-derived MOFs was further investigated for only several oxides such as V2O5, MgO, MnO, Bi2O3, CoO, NiO, and CdO. Vanadium PCPs, due to their porosity and electronic properties, can be applied in separation, sensing, and catalysis. The first approach of vanadium based polycrystalline macrostructures [V(OH)NDC]n fabricated from V2O5 was introduced by Reboul and coworkers.304 PCPs were obtained in a short 10 min microwave treatment from a water solution of V2O5 and naphthalene dicarboxylic acid (H2NDC), in the presence of ascorbic acid as a reductant. Ascorbic acid not only changed the oxidation state of vanadium from VV to VIII, but also improved the solubility of the mineral.259 A combination of applied radiation and mineral precursor ended up in covering the entire surface of the oxide with nuclei. V2O5-derived MOFs showed a rod-like shape and had smaller and more uniform particle size than VCl3-derived ones.

Wang et al.305 presented a new synthetic strategy for Mg-MOF-74 films from MgO using solvothermal conditions. Magnesium nitrate salts were in situ produced during synthesis, notwithstanding MgO was used as a seeding agent hooked onto the Al2O3 substructure. MgO released Mg2+ ions, and promoted both nucleation and growth of MOF thin films. The resulting MOF membranes demonstrated no defects in their structure, despite the presence of nitrate ions. The effect of the amount of the mineral precursor on the morphology of MOF was investigated by Maserati and his group in 2017.306 With different ratios of MgO to H4dobpdc [4,4′-dihydroxy-(1,1′-biphenyl)-3,3′-dicarboxylic acid] they presented two synthetic routes for nanocrystals (16:1) or microcrystals (2:1). In the case of not fully converted MgO to MOF, acetic acid was used as a mild etchant to separate product from MO. The final morphology of the two products was rod-like. These rods revealed an average cross-section crystallite size of ∼12 nm, and a greater surface area than bulk structures. Another interesting approach used seemingly mechanochemically inert MgO in a one step LAG method. Grinding MO with carboxylic acid drug derivatives, such as ibuprofen or salicylic acid in the presence of water led to a complex self-assembly process. It involved coordination to water molecules with subsequent acid–base neutralisation and molecular inclusion. In 2010 Chow et al.307 synthesized a series of diverse MOF networks via the same method by varying the amount of water coordinated to the Mg2+ centre. Similarly to previous structures a bismuth subsalicylate MOF was synthesized by using ion- and liquid-assisted grinding (ILAG). The environmentally friendly method using Bi2O3 as a metal source and coordinating drugs allowed preparation of novel metallodrugs. The volume of solvent had a high impact on the final network, thus hydrated 2D crystal structure varied depending on the water coordinates.308 To reduce the amount of water coordinates and to avoid temperature treatment it is feasible to conduct a LAG procedure also in non-aqueous solution e.g. in DMF. Such an example was obtained by Wang and coworkers for CdO-derived MOFs.309 Milling CdO with isonicotinic acid (HINA) led to the formation of an interpenetrated diamondoid network of [Cd(INA)2(OH2)]·DMF. MO-to-MOF conversion efficiency in this case is very small leaving a lot of unreacted CdO.

3D and mesoporous ZIF-67, Co-MOF-74 and Ni-MOF-74 hybrid arrays were successfully synthesized by Cai et al.,310 by using self-sacrificial CoO and NiO arrays on Ni foam with Hmim and 2,5-dihydroxyterephthalic acid as the organic linkers. Firstly a MO precursor on Ni foam was prepared. Next, it was placed in a mixture of water, ethanol and DMF with 2,5-dihydroxyterephthalic acid as the linker. Poor solubility of MOs at times can be still ‘too fast’, thus for the shape-preservation procedure it needs to be lowered. In this case using only water led to too fast dissociation and nucleation in the solution, whereas for morphology control epitaxial growth is required. Hence, the addition of ethanol slowed the reaction down and enabled well-defined MOF formation, meaning a slow rate of dissolution was important for morphology control for this material. ZIF-67 morphology was directed by changing the pristine shape of the mineral, e.g. applying CoO wires led to rod formation, whereas CoO walls formed CoO@ZIF-67 walls. The presented growth mechanism revealed the phenomenon of shell thickening in spite of complete core surrounding. After 12 h of reaction, the size of CoO core/ZIF-67 shell changed from 100/40 nm to 53/180 nm. Continuation of the reaction led to core corrosion, but it did not stop further ZIF-shell formation. This means that organic linkers were able to deprotonate themselves and enhance CoO dissolution; on top of that they could easily penetrate ZIF pores and add up to its structure. Interestingly, replacing CoO with Co3O4 did not lead to the formation of ZIF-67 on the oxide Co3O4.311

The solution-mediated techniques are rather expensive and environmentally unfriendly. These problems prompted Friščić et al.311,312 to develop an ‘accelerated ageing method’, which imitates geological biomineralization (mineral weathering/mineral neogenesis). MOF materials are generated from the exposure of minerals (e.g. MgO, MnO, NiO, CoO, CuO, PbO) to organic molecules (oxalate ligands) without solvent. Motillo et al.307 also introduced “accelerated aging” for ZIF-67 and ZIF-8 preparation.313,314 This technique enabled formation of ZIFs from CoO and ZnO; however, it took many days (up to 11 days in some cases) to gain any hybrid structure. Additionally, besides linkers (Hmim or HEtim), salt additives are needed, such as (NH4)2SO4, KHSO4, (H2Im)2SO4·H2O, (Hcaf)(HSO4), (Hcaf)(HSO4)·H2O or (H2BIm)2(SO4), which for the morphology control purposes can be unreasonable, if once stated acidic by-products might occur or no well-defined shape and size of the final material is obtained.

5. Controlling morphology of metal–organic framework-derived carbon

Since the pioneering work by Xu and coworkers315 in 2008, MOF-derived carbon (MDC) has been of particular research interest in the past decade.316–324 The characteristic features of MOFs, such as designable porosity and ordered structure with highly developed surface area,6,325 are partially transferred to derived carbons, so that MDCs can have tailored physicochemical properties which are difficult to realize by other porous carbons. MOFs are now considered as “all-in-one” templates/precursors that contain both inorganic and organic constituents which can be pre-selected for preparation of targeted carbon materials.326 Inorganic metal species function as sacrificial porogens, catalysts for in situ graphitization, and active components that are useful for energy storage and heterogeneous catalysis.327–329 Organic ligands serve as mainly carbon and heteroatom sources, as well as pore forming agents. They decompose to gaseous products and/or cross-link to form porous (heteroatom-doped) amorphous carbon. In situ generated gas outflux can sometimes create additional mesopores and even macropores.330–332 The well-defined periodic arrangement of metal nodes and organic motifs in MOFs often leads to a homogeneous distribution of metal or MO nanoparticles in the carbon matrix.320,333 This feature even allows preparation of single atomic metal catalysts and metal-N-C active sites throughout the whole structures, which is useful for various applications.334

MOFs can be further functionalized, either by guest incorporation into the porous MOF hosts or post-synthetic modification of MOFs, to achieve improved MDC material performance.335 For instance, secondary carbon sources (furfuryl alcohol, glucose, phenolic resins) and heteroatom sources (dicyanamide for nitrogen, glyphosine and triarylphosphine for phosphorus, and dimethyl sulfoxide for sulfur) can significantly improve the textural and electronic properties of MDCs, respectively.319 Addition of secondary metal ions and other inorganic precursors enables preparation of diverse metallic and multi-metallic nanocatalysts.320,333 The reaction between MOF and sulfur, selenium, or phosphorus containing chemicals at high temperature leads to the formation of carbon composites with metal chalcogenides and phosphides.336,337 Apart from MOF precursors, the final properties and structures of MDCs can also be controlled by simply varying the pyrolysis conditions such as temperature, ramping rate, retention time, and gas atmosphere.338–341

Another important key advantage of MDCs is controllable particle morphologies ranging from simple 0D, 1D, 2D to complex 3D and hierarchical architectures. Carbon morphology control is essential to understanding the fundamental morphology–performance relationships and therefore could provide new insights for the rational design of carbon materials that are highly desired in real world applications.342 Morphology control also expands the scope of possible applications of carbons to cutting edge biomedical, energy, and environmental technologies.316–321 In this respect, MDC is highly advantageous due to the straightforward pseudomorphic transformation of the parent MOF morphology into MDCs. In addition, complex yet well-defined 3D hierarchical architectures can be realized by MOF deposition on the external templates with predefined structures and morphologies. The pre-synthesized MOFs can also be processed into desired morphologies through various top-down fabrication techniques (refer to Section 4).

Although some excellent reviews on morphology and size control of MOFs,264,343–345 preparation of MOF-derived materials,252,337,346,347 and applications of MDCs316–321 have been published, a comprehensive overview on controlling morphologies of MDCs is still missing. This MDC section covers the carbonization mechanism, morphology control methods, and future outlook, so that general trends and future direction of MDC can be sketched. We focus on strategies for MDC morphology control, with particular emphasis on “solid metal oxides as primary architecture directing agents (i.e. reactive external templates)” for MDC preparation.

5.1. Carbonization mechanism

The main scope of this section is to provide key information on thermal transformation mechanism of MOFs into carbons upon heat treatment under an inert gas atmosphere. For the other metal-based nanomaterials, such as MOF-derived MOs, carbides, chalcogenides, and phosphides, the readers can refer to various reviews in the literature.326,337,348 The factors that affect the physicochemical properties of MDCs will be discussed with particular emphasis on the role of metal ions, organic ligands and pyrolysis conditions.

Investigating thermal stability of MOFs is the first step to understand their thermal transformation mechanisms. Thermal stability and MOF decomposition profiles were traced by thermogravimetric analysis, temperature programmed-mass spectrometry,338,350 and pyrolysis-gas chromatography/mass spectrometry.332 Zhang et al.338 systematically investigated the decomposition of MOF-5 (Zn4O(BDC)3) for the first time. The guest solvents vaporized, and then MOF-5 started to decompose at temperature >400 °C. The decomposition products were identified as CO, CO2, and benzene without any other hydrocarbons. Fig. 21a shows the proposed MOF-5 decomposition mechanism, consisting of (a) Zn–O bond breaking between the Zn4O cluster and carboxylic group, (b) O–C bond breaking of the carboxylic group, and (c) C–C bond breaking between the carboxylic group and benzene ring. MOF-5 decomposed through either (a + c) breaking (75%) or b breaking (25%). Both pathways work cooperatively to form CO2, ZnO nanoparticles, and benzene-derived carbon species, thereby resulting in ZnO/amorphous carbon composites. Carbothermal reduction of ZnO to Zn metals started at 750 °C, accompanying the release of CO and CO2 gas (ZnO + C → Zn + CO or CO2).350 Subsequent heating at higher temperature (e.g. 900 °C) led to vaporization of in situ formed Zn metals. This process is analogous to the chemical activation and produces a large number of micropores in MDCs. The kinds of metal ions and organic ligands significantly influence the coordination strength and thermal stability of MOFs, and therefore their transformation behaviors. For instance, the carbonization process of ZIF-8 – the representative example of robust MOFs with high thermal stability – begins at >600 °C, releasing methyl side groups followed by nitrogen and vaporized Zn metals at higher temperature. Thus, the framework decomposition and carbonization rate of ZIF were relatively slower than those of carboxylate ligand-based MOFs.339


Fig. 21 (a) Proposed thermal decomposition mechanism of MOF-5 (Reprinted with permission.338 Copyright 2010, American Chemical Society). (b) The effect of reduction potential of metal atoms present in the MOFs on the formation of the metal/MO nanoparticles (Reprinted with permission.349 Copyright 2012, The Royal Society of Chemistry).

Two major components of MOFs, metal ions and organic ligands work cooperatively in determining the properties of the final MDCs. Metal species act as sacrificial pore forming agents, catalysts for in situ graphitization, and active components for target applications. During the pyrolysis of MOFs, the metal ions/clusters converted to either MOs or metals. Das et al.349 suggested a standard reduction potential of metal ions as a criterion to predict formation of metal or MOs in the carbon matrix (Fig. 21b). The threshold reduction potential was proposed as −0.27 V. The metal ions (e.g. Co, Ni, Cu) with reduction potential >−0.27 V form pure metal nanoparticles, while those <−0.27 V (e.g. Mg, Al, Mn, Zn, Cr) combine with the oxygen of the organic ligands to form MO nanoparticles during thermolysis under N2 at 900 °C. The Gibbs free energy of oxidation of metals can also help to estimate the temperature needed for carbothermal reduction of specific oxides.351 The Ellingham diagram – widely used in extractive metallurgy – shows the relationship between the formation free energy of oxides versus temperature, so that the stability of oxides and the associated reduction temperature can be derived.351In situ generated metal nanoparticles play multiple roles in controlling the properties of MDCs. Volatile Zn is easily vaporized during carbonization >900 °C, leaving a number of pores (mostly micropores but even meso- and macropores) and thereby greatly increasing the porosity and specific surface area of MDCs. On the other hand, metallic Co, Ni, and Fe can induce catalytic graphitization, increasing the degree of graphitization of carbon frameworks. The controlled pyrolysis of such metal containing MOFs enabled the formation of oriented-carbon nanotube (CNT) assemblies,328 and porous graphene.352,353 To achieve synergistic effects from each metal component, bimetallic or multi-metallic MOFs were designed and used as precursors for direct preparation of functional MDCs.354,355 The residual metal species either contribute to the formation of additional pores through chemical etching356 or work as active catalysts for specific applications.327

Organic ligands work as porogens as well as carbon and heteroatom sources (e.g. imidazolate and NH2-functionalized ligands). During pyrolysis under an inert atmosphere, they decomposed to gaseous products and condensed/cross-linked to form (heteroatom-doped) amorphous carbon. This process typically leads to the formation of random mesopores in MDCs, yet can be modified to afford foam-like macroporous structures as well. Kitagawa and coworkers330 reported a ligand-assisted foaming process by using MOFs constructed by Zn2+ and ligands with nitro group substituents. During carbonization, the nitro group rapidly decomposed/released a large amount of NO2 which acts as a foaming agent to create hierarchically porous carbon networks. Recently, Zhao et al.331 reported a similar foaming effect in Zn-based triazole-rich energetic MOFs. Kim et al.332 utilized labile, thermally less stable aliphatic carboxylate ligands as mesopore-forming agents. Aliphatic ligands were first transformed into organic substances with high boiling point (even higher than 550 °C) and then subsequently evaporated to generate numerous mesopores throughout the whole carbon matrix.340,341 In addition, the inherent characteristics of ligands greatly influence the textural properties of the resulting MDCs. Kurungot and coworkers357 directly carbonized various Zn-MOFs with different ligand aromaticities, such as MOF-2, MOF-5, Zn-BTC (Zn-1,3,5-benzenetricarboxylic acid), Zn-NDC (Zn-2,6-naphthalene dicarboxylic acid), Zn-PAA (Zn-1,4-phenylenediacetic acid), and Zn-ADA (Zn-1,3-adamantanediacetic acid) and observed that the surface area of MDCs increases with increasing aromaticity of the MOF precursors.

Several attempts have been made to correlate the properties of parent MOFs with those of daughter MDCs, yet there is no general consensus on such relations. Earlier studies suggested that the porous structures and surface area of MDCs are mostly inherited from MOF precursors.316 For instance, Lim et al.358 carbonized a series of Zn based MOFs with different structures and compositions, and found a linear correlation between the Zn/C ratio of MOF precursors and the surface area of the resulting MDCs (i.e. the higher the Zn/C ratio, the larger the surface area). However, this finding was difficult to be generalized, because many other factors (metal ions, ligands, bond strength, framework topology, MOF preparation methods) were not considered. Recently, Moon and coworkers326 made a more comprehensive survey of the literature, and found that there are no apparent trends between the surface area of MOFs and MDCs, nor between the Zn/C ratio of MOFs and the surface area of MDCs.

Instead, it was suggested that the physicochemical properties of MDCs are highly dependent on the pyrolysis conditions (temperature, ramping rate, and retention time).326 Indeed, the same parent MOFs can be transformed into daughter carbons with significantly different physicochemical properties by controlling the pyrolysis conditions, in agreement with a number of previous literature.315,339,356,359–361 The high temperature carbonization could enhance the porosity at the expense of carbon yield and loss of heteroatom species. In addition, as demonstrated by Hwang et al.,340 even the nonporous MOFs can be converted to MDCs with controllable large surface area, nitrogen content and pore structures by simply varying the pyrolysis temperature (550–1000 °C) and the removal method of in situ generated Zn porogens. The porosity of MDCs can also be tuned by changing the ramping rates and retention time.341 Slow ramping rate and long retention time at 750 °C (5 °C min−1, 6 h) promoted the aggregation of Zn species which contribute to increase in mesopore volume. Fast ramping rate and short retention time at 1000 °C (10 °C min−1, 1 h) rapidly removed in situ generated Zn metal, leading to the formation of micropore-dominant carbon.

The carbonization mechanisms are highly dependent on the kinds of metal ion–organic ligand pairs and their coordination strength, as well as pyrolysis conditions. The physicochemical properties of daughter MDCs are not only partially inherited from parent MOFs but also greatly affected by the multiple factors as aforementioned. The relationship between the nature of the parent MOF and that of daughter MDC still remains rather controversial. Therefore, it is highly recommended to investigate the underlying thermal transformation mechanisms to prepare MDCs with desired properties (e.g. surface area, pore size, pore volume, degree of graphitization, and heteroatom doping level). The universal trends/rules that govern the thermal transformation process and associated pore formation mechanism are ongoing subjects of research.

5.2. Strategies for morphology control of MOF-derived carbon

We categorize the reported MDC literature into the following four general morphology control strategies: (1) MOF itself as a self-template, (2) non-reactive external template method, (3) reactive external template method, and (4) top-down fabrication method (Fig. 22 and Table 3). In the MOF self-template method, the morphologies of parent MOFs are inherited to daughter MDCs without the assistance of other templates or microfabrication techniques. Higher level of morphology control can be achieved by integrating MOFs with external templates with predefined structures and morphologies. This external template method can be further divided into two categories, non-reactive and reactive ones. The former mainly relies on the coordination between the surface functional groups of templates and the metal ions; thus surface modification is usually required to induce preferential MOF deposition on non-reactive templates. The latter uses insoluble solid matters (e.g. MOs) as reactive self-sacrificial templates. The solid MOs function as metal ion reservoirs and specific nucleation sites, which directly react with the solid/liquid/gas phase organic ligands at the interfaces. Finally, the pre-synthesized MOFs are utilized as building blocks for various top-down processing techniques to prepare MOF/MDC with desired morphologies. Based on these four categories, we further sub-divided the MDCs according to their dimensions, i.e. 0D (dot, polyhedra, hollow- and multi-shelled polyhedra), 1D (rods and tubes), 2D (sheets, ribbons, sandwiches), 3D and hierarchical morphologies (arrays, superstructures, hierarchical architectures), and discuss their formation mechanism as well as their thermal transformation process. We place particular emphasis on ‘solid MOs as reactive external templates’, and comprehensively review the available literature on this strategy as a versatile tool to obtain complex yet sophisticated carbon architectures with multiple levels of hierarchies (see also Section 4).
Fig. 22 Strategies for morphology control of MOF-derived carbon. (a) MOF self-template method, (b) non-reactive external template method, (c) reactive external template method and (d) top-down fabrication.
Table 3 Synthetic strategies for MDC nanostructures with controlled morphology

• Insufficient control of MOF thickness
• Only a few MOFs can be directly grown on the non-reactive external templates, and their detailed growth mechanism is not fully understood
Reactive external template • Reactive external template provides additional advantages as well as those of non-reactive external template • Time-consuming multi-step procedures (0D) hollow sphere, hollow polyhedral ZnO, CoO, NiO, Cu(OH)2, cobalt oxalate, cobalt carbonate hydroxide, PAN/metal acetate, layered double-metal hydroxide, and solid precursors grown on carbon cloth, metal foam/mesh/foil, fluorine doped tin oxide glass 281, 310, 336, 408, 418–427 and 429–438
• Reactive external templates function as architecture directing agent, cation reservoirs, and specific nucleation sites at the same time • The balance between the release rate of metal ions from the solid matters and the coordination rate of metal ions with ligands should be controlled (1D) wires, tubes, hierarchical tubes
• Additional metal ion source and surface functionalization are not needed • The reaction parameters should be tuned to attain controllable nucleation and growth on templates (2D) sheets, honeycomb-like flakes, plates
• The possibility of creating complex morphologies with multiple levels of structural hierarchies which are difficult to obtain from the other MOF-mediated routes • Inhomogeneous MOF deposition (formation of undesired bulk MOFs in solution) (3D) flowers, urchin-like, arrays (wires, tubes grown on conductive substrates)
• Well-established morphology control of MOs can be transferred to MDCs • Insufficient control of MOF thickness
• Cheap solid precursors from large-scale industrial production or earth-abundant natural minerals are available • Chemical composition of parent MOs is restricted to a few substances, mostly Zn and Co based ones
• Considerable lack of understanding of underlying growth kinetics and thermodynamics
Top-down fabrication • A wide variety of techniques (chemical etching, exfoliation, microfabrication such as electrospinning and 3D printing) are available • Chemical etching is dependent on the stability of specific MOFs against the etchant solution (0D) hollow polyhedra, adamantine-like Chemically etched ZIF-8 and ZIF-67, electrospun ZIF-8, ZIF-67, and MIL-88, exfoliated MOFs, 3D printed Co-MOF 323, 324 and 441–452
• Pre-synthesized MOFs can be processed into desired morphologies • Exfoliations are only applicable to a few layer-structured MOFs; construction of high-quality 2D MOFs and their conformal transformation are challenging (1D) rods, tubes
• Separation of the MOF preparation and the MOF shaping • Development of low cost, high yield, and versatile top-down methods is highly desired (2D) sheet, paper, necklace-like paper, stacks, nanoribbon
• Any kinds of MOFs with various combinations can be applied in microfabrication (3D) printed mesh architectures
• The possibility of obtaining MDCs with unique structures and morphologies

5.2.1. Conformal transformation of MOF self-template. The inherent crystal morphologies of MOFs can be transferred to the derived MDCs, namely, conformal transformation or pseudomorphic transformation. The morphologies of MDCs are inherited from MOFs without the use of additional templates and processing techniques, i.e. the MOF self-templating method. This approach is simple, straightforward and economical. Despite considerable progress in morphology and size control of MOFs,343 morphologies of MDCs are still rather restricted to a few simple single crystal morphologies such as polyhedra, rods, and sheets.
Zero-dimensional carbons. Direct carbonization of MOFs can produce MDCs with different 0D morphologies such as polyhedra, dots, and hollow and multi-shelled structures. Polyhedral ZIFs are by far the most widely used MOFs for conformal transformation because of their high thermal stability.339,365 Thus, most common morphologies of MDCs are polyhedra (hexahedral, octahedral, and dodecahedral shape). Carbon dots can also be prepared by pyrolysis of small ZIF-8 nanoparticles of <20 nm at 500 °C in Ar flow for 10 h.366

Particular attention has been paid to hollow and multi-shelled MDCs.367 ZIF-67 is commonly used as a single precursor for direct preparation of hollow graphitic carbons. The formation of highly active Co metal nanocatalysts via controlling the pyrolysis temperature and gas atmosphere is the key to obtain hollow N-doped CNT particles (N-CNTs). Xia et al.368 converted ZIF-67 to hollow N-CNTs through pyrolysis under a reducing H2/N2 gas atmosphere in a wide temperature range (600–900 °C). H2 rapidly generated highly active Co metal nanoparticles which catalyzed the growth of N-CNTs. Because of the preferential surface-enrichment of the Co metal, the subsequent catalytic growth direction of N-CNTs is from inside to outside, resulting in the formation of hollow structures. Controlled low temperature pyrolysis of various Co-MOFs near the decomposition temperature of MOFs (430 °C) for 8 h under Ar flow led to hollow N-CNT assemblies.328In situ generated reducing gas turned the surface Co ions/clusters to uniformly distributed metallic Co nanoparticles which resulted in similar inside-to-outside growth of N-CNTs.

Heterogeneous contraction during thermal treatment is a simple and efficient strategy for preparation of hollow particles.367 Liu et al.362 synthesized hollow N-doped microporous carbon@Co-N-doped graphitic carbon (NC@Co-NGC) nanocages via external rigid-shell-induced outward contraction (Fig. 23a). The difference in thermal stability between ZIF-8 core and ZIF-67 shell (ZIF-8@ZIF-67) precursor is the key factor. The less stable ZIF-67 shell decomposed first to form rigid shells of CoO/carbon around ZIF-8 cores above 500 °C. The strong connection between CoO/carbon and ZIF-8 provided an outward adhesive force to pull the ZIF-8-derived carbon to adhere on the CoO/carbon shell. Following carbonization at 800 °C and catalytic CNT growth on Co metals resulted in hollow nanocages with NC@Co-NGC shells (Fig. 23b–e). This strategy is also applicable to the other types of rigid shells, such as silica369 and polymers.370 Recently, multilayer solid ZIFs, prepared by stepwise multiple epitaxial growths on preformed ZIF-seed, were converted to multishell hollow metal@NC with precisely controlled shell number and composition.371


Fig. 23 Carbon derived from MOF self-templates. (a–e) 0D hollow N-doped microporous carbon@Co-N-doped graphitic carbon (NC@Co-NGC) nanocages prepared by external rigid-shell-induced outward contraction (Reproduced with permission.362 Copyright 2017, John Wiley & Sons), (f) solvent mediated selective synthesis of 1D and 2D MOFs and their transformation to nanoporous carbons (licensed under CC-BY363), (g) surfactant assisted synthesis of 2D porphyrin paddlewheel framework-3 (PPF-3) nanosheets and their derived CoS/N-doped carbon nanosheets and (h and i) the corresponding TEM images of the sample (Reproduced with permission.364 Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society).
One-dimensional carbons. MOFs with intrinsic 1D crystal morphologies were the straightforward choice of precursors for carbon nanorods and nanotubes. However, there are only a few 1D MOFs available, such as M-BTC (M = Mn, Zn),372,373 Fe-MIL-88,374 and Al-MOF (Al(OH)(1,4-NDC)·2H2O).329 Interestingly, carbonization and HF etching of Al-MOF led to sponge-like carbon nanorods made up of randomly assembled nanosheets having tunable large surface area and pore volume.329

Anisotropic 1D MOFs can be prepared by a coordination modulation in which certain monodentate modulators (e.g. monocarboxylic acid) competitively interact with organic ligands and by that change the coordination equilibrium and promote/suppress the relative growth rates of MOF-crystal faces.375 Zn-MOF-74 nanorods were synthesized by using salicylic acid as a coordination modulator, and then conformally transformed to 1D carbon nanorods.376 Different from conventional modulators, the solvent itself can function as both a structure directing agent isolating particular MOF polymorph and a crystal modulator controlling the aspect ratio of nanorods (Fig. 23f).363 In an ambient temperature synthesis of [Zn2(BDC)2DABCO]n (DABCO = 1,4-diazabicyclo[2.2.2]octane), dimethylformamide (DMF) and methanol induced the selective formation of the kinetic polymorph with a 1D hexagonal rod morphology. Addition of methanol decreased the rate of nucleation and thus led to the formation of larger rod-MOFs. Methanol also suppressed the crystal growth along Zn-DABCO [001] directions by forming hydrogen bonds with the nucleophilic DABCO ligand, thereby inducing the morphology change from 1D hexagonal rods to 2D nanoplatelets. Thus, 1D hexagonal rods with different size and aspect ratio can be easily prepared. The structure formation also has a significant effect on electrochemical applications (refer to Section 6).

Two-dimensional carbons. 2D MOFs are usually prepared by top-down (exfoliation of layered bulk MOFs; kindly refer to Section 5.2.4) or bottom-up approaches (surfactant or coordination modulator-assisted anisotropic crystal growth). Only a few 2D MOFs, such as ZIF-L with 2D leaf-like morphology (a polymorph of ZIF-8)377 and recently developed 2D Zn-hexamine coordination frameworks378 were employed as precursors for N-doped porous carbon nanosheets.

Surfactants and coordination modulators have been widely used to regulate the growth of bulk crystals into anisotropic 2D morphologies. Cao et al.364 reported a surfactant assisted synthesis of 2D porphyrin paddlewheel framework-3 (PPF-3) nanosheets and their derived CoS/N-doped carbon nanosheets (Fig. 23g–i). PVP attached on the surface of MOF, promoted the anisotropic growth of MOFs and finally formation of nanosheets with tunable thickness. Because of relatively low thermal stability and significant framework shrinkage during carbonization, the 2D square-like morphology was only retained when sufficiently thick (ca. 40 nm) MOF nanosheets were used. Ultrathin PPF-3 <10 nm was completely destroyed after carbonization. However, this approach may not be suitable for large-scale synthesis because of the high cost of porphyrin ligands. He and coworkers379 developed a high-yield (>65%) synthesis of ultrathin MOF/MDC nanosheets (ca. 2.5 nm) by using low-cost ligand sources (benzimidazole and sodium acetate). Sodium gluconate modulators restricted the growth of the layered MOFs in the direction perpendicular to the layers. Likewise, Mn-BDC nanosheets were prepared by simply adding monodentate benzoate modulator into the synthesis solution of bulk Mn-BDC cubes.380

Three-dimensional and hierarchical carbon. For preparation of 3D and hierarchical architectures, several novel approaches based on controlling the thermal transformation process and MOF crystallization pathway have been reported in recent years. Xiong and coworkers381 reported the formation of 3D dandelion-like CoO@C spheres through the pyrolysis of metal–organic layers with 2D framework topologies. Upon thermal annealing, the ligand-bridges decomposed to form 1D chains of CoO6 octahedra which in turn converted to 1D nanowires. Meanwhile, morphology evolution proceeded from flake-like crystals to dandelion-like spheres composed of 1D nanowire arrays through thermally induced recrystallization and self-assembly. However, this approach needs a specific MOF with special 2D topologies and therefore lacks versatility as it is not applicable to other MOFs.

Guest incorporation can change thermal transformation mechanisms and create new synergistic effects that induce dramatic morphological transformation. Guest@MOF has shown great potential as a versatile precursor to achieve hierarchical carbon structures. In 2016, Zhu et al.383 explored this concept for the first time, and developed honeycomb-like porous carbons with embedded Co9S8 nanoparticles. Decomposition of encapsulated thiourea and cobalt chloride within the MOF not only led to the formation of Co9S8 nanoparticles but also released a large amount of N, S-containing gases which induced heteroatom doping and expansion of the pores. Smoukov and coworkers384 found that carbonization of molybdate@HKUST-1 yielded natural diatom-like carbons with multi-level hierarchies, (i) outermost polyhedral boundaries (15 μm) composed of (ii) webs (0.5–1 μm), (iii) fibres inside the diatom (100 nm in diameter), and (iv) mesopores (20 nm). Upon pyrolysis, Cu migrated to the outer surface of carbons due to weak Cu–C interactions, while Mo stayed in the carbon matrix due to strong Mo–C interactions. A small fraction of Cu in proximity to Mo can synergistically catalyze the growth of web- and fibre-like carbon nanostructures, which cannot be achieved by Cu or Mo alone. Thus, the fibres grew only inside of the carbon cages. The surface accumulated Cu could not contribute to fibre formation and left mesopores after acid washing.

Of particular interest in this research area is the development of hierarchical carbons with well-defined 3D superstructures, which can show novel collective properties that are considerably better than the sum of their parts. However, the examples as such are still very rare. Xu and coworkers382 fabricated a spherical superstructure of carbon nanorods with hierarchical pores (Fig. 24a). Under a hydrothermal reaction condition, the presynthesized spherical Zn-MOF-74 (Zn2(C8H4O6)) nanoparticles were firstly transformed into Zn(H2O)2(C8H4O6) nanorods having different crystal structures, and then self-organized to chestnut-like-superstructures. The use of Zn-MOF-74 (a precursor), water (a solvent), and urea (a crystal modulator) was critical for controlled dissolution and recrystallization to obtain the final 3D superstructures with radially oriented 1D nanorods (Fig. 24b–e). Very recently, Hwang et al.340 reported hierarchically porous 3D flower-like carbon superstructures through direct thermal transformation of bio-inspired MOF mesocrystals (Fig. 24f). The initially formed 2D nanosheet acted as a primary building block that underwent oriented attachment and mesoscale assembly. The secondary nucleation and growth on the preformed layers resulted in the formation of 3D flower-like mesocrystals composed of 2D nanosheets. Nitric acid-mediated kinetics control enabled the morphogenesis from 2D nanosheets to 3D flower-like superstructures with controllable particle size and dimensions (Fig. 24g–j). Finally, the mesocrystals were converted into porous carbon materials.


Fig. 24 3D carbon derived from MOF self-templates. (a) Fabrication of spherical carbon nanorod superstructures (SS-CNR). (b and c) SEM and (d and e) TEM images of SS-CNR (Reproduced with permission.382 Copyright 2019, John Wiley & Sons). (f) Suggested formation of flower Zn-NTA (nitrilotriacetic acid) mesocrystals. (g–j) SEM images of Zn-NTA mesocrystals prepared under different HNO3/NTA (r) (Reproduced with permission.340 Copyright 2019, John Wiley & Sons).
Challenges and opportunities. The use of MOFs as the sole precursor for MDCs still suffers from the following problems. High temperature thermal transformation often leads to a partial or complete collapse of the original MOF morphology, and is sometimes accompanied by particle agglomerations and loss of ordered pore structure. These drawbacks could be partially overcome by stabilizing MOFs with the well-defined external templates, which will be discussed in the following sections. In this regard, development of thermally stable MOFs with various compositions and structures (other than ZIFs) is highly desired. Furthermore, direct fabrication of MOF with multiple levels of structural hierarchy still remains a great challenge because of the single crystalline nature of MOFs and incomplete understanding of their crystallization mechanism. In a classical crystallization theory, the crystal morphologies likely reflect the replication/amplification of the unit cells. Although the SBU concept has been well established to predict/determine the framework topologies, SBUs cannot be simply considered as growth units because they are oriented in the network in a complicated way.385 Therefore, a further understanding of a standard growth unit of MOFs and associated crystallization mechanism is highly required.

To circumvent this issue, a nonclassical crystallization pathway for morphology control of MOFs has been recently proposed.340,386 Natural mineralization often follows this pathway to create sophisticated 3D mesocrystals via particle-mediated growth and attachment, as observed in various biominerals (e.g. bone, nacre, sea urchins).387,388 Mesocrystal is a kind of superstructure consisting of individual nanoparticles in the crystallographic register with defined order.387,388 Inspired by nature, Schmidt and coworkers386 reported double hydrophilic block copolymer-mediated morphogenesis of MOF mesocrystals with high level of structure control, such as kinetic formation of MOF mesocrystals and their recrystallization to MOF mesocrystals with a thermodynamic structure, as well as hybridization of two distinct MOFs. This approach opens a new avenue for morphogenesis of MOFs and thus possibly MDCs. Along this line, we also believe that in situ hybridization of MOF and other components into single architectures (e.g. MOF–MOF, MOF-polymers)355,389–391 is a promising strategy for morphology control of MDCs, by controlling the MOF architectures themselves and/or by changing the thermal transformation process.

5.2.2. Non-reactive external templates. In situ growth of MOFs on external templates can mitigate some of the inherent drawbacks of the MOF self-template method. The most obvious advantage of the external template is the straightforward control over the size and morphology of resulting carbon materials. In addition, MDC can remain relatively well-dispersed without significant aggregation because the parent MOFs are anchored and stabilized on the external templates during thermal transformation. External templates either generate internal voids through their removal or contribute as functional components in the final MDC composites. In the case of a non-reactive external template, surface modification is usually required to increase the interaction/affinity with metal ions and therefore to achieve a homogeneous and uniform MOF coating. For example, functionalization with carboxyl/sulfonyl groups or polymers (e.g. PVP) is conducted before MOF deposition.
Zero-dimensional carbons. Typically, carboxylate (or PVP)-terminated poly(styrene) (PS) beads392 were mixed with a solution of metal ions/organic ligands to obtain core–shell PS@MOF microspheres (Fig. 25a). Surface carboxyl groups interacted with metal ions and initiated the growth of MOFs. The resulting PS@MOF hybrids were subjected to carbonization under an inert gas atmosphere, during which the MOF shell transformed into porous carbon and the PS core decomposed to form hollow interiors. Several PS@ZIF-8,392,396 PS@ZIF-67,397 PS@MIL-101/ZIF67398 were used for the preparation of hollow carbon spheres with or without metal nanoparticles embedded in the carbon shell. Interestingly, fast pyrolysis of PS@ZIF-67 at a rapid heating rate (>5 °C min−1) yielded “single-holed” hollow carbon spheres.397 Thermal decomposition of the PS core generated a strong hydrocarbon gas outflux which created a large single hole in each shell. Zhang et al.398 prepared MIL-101/ZIF-67 composites shells on PS spheres (PS@MIL-101/ZIF-67) by functionalizing the surfaces of both PS spheres and MIL-101(Fe) nanoparticles with PVP. Thus, the preformed secondary MIL-101 could be homogeneously distributed within the dual-MOF nanoshells. The subsequent pyrolysis produced CoFe alloy@N-doped hollow carbon spheres.
Fig. 25 Carbon derived from MOF/non-reactive external templates. (a) Schematic representation for synthesis of 0D hollow carbon from PS@ZIF-8 (Reprinted with permission.392 Copyright 2014, The Royal Chemical Society). (b) 1D carbon coated Mo-based nanocomposites from PVP-modified MoO3@bimetallic ZIFs (Reproduced with permission.393 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society). (c) 2D porous carbon nanosheets from ZIF/GO sandwiches and (d and e) their TEM images (Reproduced with permission.394 Copyright 2015, John Wiley & Sons). (f) Schematic diagram for 3D ordered macroporous ZIF-8 single crystal-derived nitrogen-doped hierarchical porous carbon and corresponding SEM (g and h) and TEM (i and j) images at different magnifications (Reprinted with permission.395 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society).

One-dimensional carbons. 1D template-directed growth of MOFs and their transformation into 1D carbon nanofibres and hollow nanotubes were demonstrated. Yu and coworkers399 utilized ultrathin tellurium nanowires (TeNWs) as hard templates to induce in situ growth and assembly of ZIF-8. Without surface modification, TeNWs were directly immersed in methanolic Zn2+/2-methylimidazole (Hmim) solution at ambient temperature, leading to the formation of TeNWs@ZIF-8. The concentrations of TeNWs and reactants were precisely controlled to inhibit solution phase growth of bulk ZIF-8. Porous carbon tubules were obtained after pyrolysis at 1000 °C, during which TeNWs were completely vaporized to form hollow interiors. ZIF-8 derived carbons can also be grown on CNTs.400 For controllable ZIF-8 deposition, CNTs were treated with a mixture of nitric acid and sulfuric acid at 80 °C to generate surface carboxylic groups which serve as nucleation sites for ZIF-8 growth. Subsequent pyrolysis at 1000 °C yielded N-doped porous carbons/CNTs with controllable large surface area. By using PVP-modified MoO3 nanorods as templates, Tian et al.393 synthesized a series of 1D Mo-based carbon nanorods and nanotubes (Fig. 25b). Amide carbonyl groups of PVP can form weak coordination interactions with metal ions in solution and allow uniform ZIF coating on MoO3 nanorods. Controlled pyrolysis at different temperatures resulted in either carbothermal reduction to form MoO2/C or a reaction with carbon to form Mo2C/C nanorods. The inner MoO2 species were dissolved and dispersed in the porous carbon matrix by HCl washing, leading to the formation of hollow MoO2/C nanotubes.
Two-dimensional carbons. Scalable synthesis of high quality 2D MOFs has been considered as a difficult task and thus only a few 2D MOFs are available for preparation of MDCs so far. This problem can be largely alleviated by using 2D external templates. Graphene has been most extensively used as a 2D template because of its excellent thermal, electrical, and mechanical properties. As nicely reviewed by Zheng et al.401 and Liu et al.,402 a wide variety of MOF/graphene composites have been prepared to combine the advantages of individual components.
