ZIF-derived graphene based N-doped porous carbon was prepared by the in situ growth of ZIF-8 on graphene oxide (GO) sheets.403 PVP functionalization facilitated the uniform nucleation of ZIF-8, resulting in the formation of dispersive 2D sandwich-like ZIF-8/GO composites. Without the use of PVP, the surface deposition of ZIF-8 was incomplete and inhomogeneous. Excess Hmim ligand was also necessary for the uniform ZIF growth on GO. Finally, thermal annealing of ZIF-8/GO at 800 °C in Ar flow generated N-doped porous carbon sheets.

ZIF-8/GO sandwiches can also be prepared by controlling the addition sequence of the precursors without any surface modification of GO (Fig. 25c–e).394 In the proposed synthetic route, GO dispersion was added to Zn ions/Hmim ligands solution where ZIF clusters were already formed. The cluster readily attached to the GO layer due to a strong interaction between metal ions of cluster and the oxygen functional groups from GO. The uniform deposition of clusters and their confined growth to ZIF nanocrystals on the GO surface allowed formation of ZIF-8/GO sandwiches with ZIF nanocrystals (20 nm) fully covering the GO. ZIF-derived carbon acted as a spacer to alleviate the significant agglomeration of GO during high-temperature treatment. In contrast, when Zn salts in methanol were firstly added to the GO solution, the Zn ions were located both on GO and in the solution because of insufficient numbers of oxygen groups in GO to capture all the Zn ions. This inhomogeneous nucleation resulted in the mixture of discrete ZIF-8 crystals and ZIF-8/GO composites.


Three-dimensional and hierarchical carbon. 3D carbon arrays, derived from MOF nanowires grown on various conductive substrates (e.g. metal foam/mesh/foil, carbon cloth, and CNT fibres) are the most common examples of external templated materials.404–407 The substrates provide additional functions such as high electronic conductivity, mechanical stability, and flexibility. Such 3D aligned architectures are of particular interest in electrochemical applications, as they can provide large electrochemical surface area which facilitates electrolyte penetration and diffusion of ionic species, and avoid the use of extra polymeric binders or conductive additives.408 In 2014, Qiao and coworkers404 fabricated MOF-derived Co3O4/carbon porous nanowire arrays for the first time. Co-NDC with a layered crystalline structure was directly grown on Cu foil through a hydrothermal reaction at 80 °C for 20 h, and then converted to Co3O4/carbon hybrid arrays with a high surface area (251 m2 g−1) and large carbon content (52.1 wt%) via pyrolysis at 600 °C under a N2 atmosphere. Inspired by this pioneering work, researchers have developed a wide variety of 3D carbon-based arrays by employing different combinations of substrates and MOFs.405–407 Instead of arrays, a surface-mounted MOF (SURMOF), a typical MOF thin film prepared by a layer-by-layer growth process, was employed as a precursor for 3D hierarchical carbon.409 A ZIF-67 thin film was grown on hydroxyl-group-terminated 3D macroporous melamine sponge and then converted to vertically aligned N-doped CNTs via pyrolysis at various temperatures in the range 700–1000 °C.

Preformed MOFs are also promising external templates that enrich the possible particle morphologies of MDCs. Different from typical MOF-on-MOF epitaxial growth, PVP-modified MOFs can be directly integrated with other MOFs via in situ assembly and growth. Guan et al.410 introduced PVP-functionalized MIL-88B (Fe) nanorods into the reactant solution of ZIF-8, and obtained uniform MIL-88B@ZIF-8 particles in which a number of 1D MIL-88B nanorods were co-assembled and encapsulated in ZIF-8 polyhedron (i.e. MOF-in-MOF). This dual-MOF with tailored morphologies and compositions enabled preparation of hierarchically structured Fe3C@N-CNT assemblies confined in a porous carbon matrix.

Shen et al.411 developed a novel approach toward single-crystal ordered macroporous ZIF-8 (Fig. 25f). ZIF-8 precursors (Zn salt and Hmim) were filled into 3D ordered PS sphere-monoliths, and then soaked in a CH3OH/NH3·H2O mixed solvent. NH3·H2O induced rapid crystallization of the precursors while CH3OH stabilized the precursors and adjusted the balance between nucleation versus growth of ZIF-8. 3D-ordered macroporous (3DOM) ZIF-8 was liberated from PS templates by repeated soaking in tetrahydrofuran. PS templates imposed unique shaping and templating effects which guided the morphological evolution of 3DOM-ZIF-8 into a tetrakaidekahedron, in contrast to typical rhombic dodecahedra. In the following work, Zhou et al.395 reported 3DOM-ZIF-8-derived hierarchically porous N-doped carbons as high performance anode materials in potassium ion batteries (Fig. 25g–j).


Challenges and opportunities. Non-reactive external template methods usually rely on time-consuming and laborious multi-step procedures, including preparation of templates (sometimes their bottom-up assembly into specific shape), surface modification, MOF deposition, carbonization, and template removal (if necessary). Another challenge in this process is the uniform MOF deposition with a controlled thickness. Non-reactive external templates lack the interaction sites that can tightly bind the metal ions. Part of adsorbed metal ions can diffuse into the ligand solution to form bulk MOFs. Thus, it is difficult to confine heterogeneous growth on the surface of templates exclusively. To date, only a few MOFs can be directly coated on the templates and their detailed growth mechanism is not fully understood yet. Thus, the exploration of new synthetic approaches for direct construction of MOFs on external templates will be the important subject of future research. In addition, development of new templates with predesigned morphologies and multi-level hierarchical structures (e.g. reactive external templates) is of urgent importance.
5.2.3. Reactive external templates. Pre-shaped, solid state MOs can be transformed into morphology conserved carbons via MOF as intermediate precursors (i.e. reactive external template method or solid-to-MOF-to-carbon pathway).259 Different from typically used solvent-soluble metal salts, the solvent-insoluble solid precursors can perform multiple roles in MOF/carbon synthesis: (i) architecture directing agents (templates): the morphologies of solid precursors are replicated to the derived MOF and the final carbon materials; (ii) cation reservoirs (metal ion source): solid precursors provide metal ions by slowly dissolving themselves with the assistance of solvents and organic ligands under specific conditions, or directly react with organic ligands under solvent-free conditions. Thus, an additional metal ion source is not needed; (iii) specific reaction sites for heterogeneous nucleation: the growth of MOF/carbon is localized/confined on the surface of solid precursors. Tedious surface modification steps typically needed in non-reactive external template methods are no longer required; (iv) functional components enhancing physical/chemical properties of the final carbon products. Typical synthesis procedures consist of mainly three steps: preparation of shaped-MOs, conversion to desired MOFs by reacting with organic ligands, and thermal transformation into carbon or carbon-based composites.

The key advantage of the reactive template method (e.g. MOs as self-sacrificing templates) lies in its ability to create complex morphologies with multiple levels of structural hierarchies. There are a wide variety of well-established approaches for controlling morphologies of MOs from nano- to micro- to macro-scopic length scales (refer to Section 3).209,210,412–417 In addition, the MOs can be directly grown onto various functional substrates and host materials, and pre-installed on specific locations before their transformation into MOFs. These capabilities enable the preparation of patterned MOFs in a spatially controlled manner, which in turn leads to the formation of sophisticated, higher-order carbon architectures such as 3D arrays and multi-dimensional hierarchical assemblies.


Zero-dimensional carbons. Song et al.418 demonstrated a synthesis of 0D hollow Co and N co-doped carbon nanopolyhedra (H-CoNC) by using ZnO nanospheres as self-sacrificing templates. Zn/Co-ZIF shell epitaxially grew on the surface of ZnO nanospheres in a mixed solvent of DMF/H2O (3:1 v/v ratio) at 50 °C. The ZnO@ZIF precursors were converted to H-CoNC after carbonization at 900 °C in a N2 atmosphere and HCl washing. For comparison, solid Co and N-codoped carbon nanopolyhedra (S-CoNCs) without a hollow cavity were prepared by pyrolysis of conventional bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIF (Fig. 26). It was revealed that the ZnO mediated-strategy had several advantages over the template-free conformal transformation. In situ evaporation of the excess Zn species during pyrolysis can (i) etch the carbon shell and enlarge the pores, leading to the formation of a well-developed hierarchical pore system ranging from 9 nm to 290 nm; (ii) help to retain more Co and N contents in carbon frameworks. In addition, the excess Zn supply could break the tradeoff between Zn and Co contents, which is observed in conventional S-CoNC. Thus, the Co and N contents increased with an increase of the ZnO/ZIF mass ratio; and (iii) allow more homogeneous Co–Nx distributions throughout the structures. The XPS depth profile analysis revealed that the chemical distributions of Co and N in H-CoNC remained constant from the outer shell to inner core, whereas a concentration gradient was observed in S-CoNC. As a result, H-CoNC showed excellent materials properties superior to those of S-CoNC; for instance, enlarged hierarchical pores (9–290 vs. 20–160 nm), high Co (0.97 vs. 0.35 wt%) and nitrogen contents (9.75 vs. 3.24 at%), and homogeneously distributed Co-Nx sites. Similarly, Chai et al.419 prepared hollow N-doped carbon materials through multiple transformation steps from amorphous Zn-BTC coordination polymers to ZnO microspheres to ZnO@ZIF-8 to final hollow N-doped carbon spheres. Hollow carbons with 25 nm-thin walls composed of highly porous carbon polyhedra were elaborately constructed.
Fig. 26 0D ZnO nanosphere directed synthesis of S-CoNC and H-CoNC (Reproduced with permission.418 Copyright 2017, John Wiley & Sons).

The direct chemical bonding of organic ligands and MOs under ‘solvent-free’ conditions has been developed to prepare carbon coated MOs. Mai and coworkers420 reported SnO2/Co@carbon via a thermal solid phase reaction of hollow CoSn(OH)6 cubes and excess 1,4-benzenedicarboxylic acid. The coordination reaction occurred at the exterior surface of the CoSn(OH)6 cubes at 300 °C, and the unreacted organic ligands vaporized at 320 °C under air. The uniformly coated MOFs were converted to carbon shells after pyrolysis under an Ar atmosphere. Similarly, N-rich carbon wrapped CoSnO3 particles were prepared by heating a mixture of CoSnO3 and Hmim in a Teflon-lined autoclave at 200 °C for 24 h without the solvents.421 Upon heating, the Hmim was melted and impregnated into the CoSnO3, and reacted with Co2+ to form ZIF-67 at both internal and external surfaces of the CoSnO3 nanoboxes. The original morphologies were well-retained after MOF growth and carbonization at 500 °C under an Ar atmosphere. As the amount of Hmim increased, the carbon (7.5–24.6 at%) and nitrogen contents (2.4–7.6 at%) increased accordingly. This strategy eliminates the solvent-related synthesis steps (e.g. washing, separation, drying) and associated problems (e.g. uncontrollable MOF growth in the bulk solution), therefore could be an attractive alternative for MOF/carbon coating on various MO substrates.


One-dimensional carbons. 1D N-doped porous carbon nanotubes with embedded core–shell Co@CoOx nanoparticles (Co@CoOx/NCNTs) were prepared by one step carbonization of bimetallic ZnO@Zn/Co-ZIF nanowires at 900 °C for 2 h under a N2 atmosphere (Fig. 27).422 Here the ZnO nanowires (ZnONWs) acted as templates, Zn2+ ion sources and specific nucleation sites for bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIF growth. In addition, in situ evaporation of Zn during pyrolysis mitigated the undesired aggregation of the carbon particles and generated hierarchical pore structures as well as tubular morphology. Notably, ZnONWs also played a crucial role in the in situ formation of Co@CoOx nanoparticles by providing excess oxygen species which directly reacted with metallic Co nanoparticles during one-step pyrolysis. In conventional ZIF pyrolysis, such Co@CoOx core–shell structures can be prepared only after an additional heat treatment under an oxidizing gas atmosphere.
Fig. 27 1D ZnO nanowire directed synthesis of Co@CoOx/NCNTs. The magnified view indicates the existence of core–shell Co@CoOx nanoparticles embedded in the porous carbon nanotube (Reprinted with permission.422 Copyright 2017, The Royal Society of Chemistry).

Other types of metal containing 1D-solid matter have been employed for directing the growth of MOF and carbonaceous materials. Starting from cobalt oxalate microtubes, Ahn et al.423 synthesized Co- and N-doped 1D carbon microtubes composed of hollow nanospheres and CNTs. Due to the presence of oxalate species, the formation of ZIF-67 nanocrystals was largely reduced and was confined in the cobalt oxalate microtubes. The cobalt species selectively reacted with Hmim in an ethanol solution for various conversion times, which greatly affected the final tubular morphologies. As the conversion time increased from 4 to 24 h, the diameter, the surface roughness, and the aspect ratio of carbon microtubes increased. In situ formed Co metallic species during pyrolysis at 800 °C catalyzed the growth of tiny CNTs, and left behind hollow nanospheres after HCl etching.

Chen et al.424 used electrospun poly(acrylonitrile) (PAN)/cobalt acetate (Co(Ac)2) nanofibres for fabrication of hierarchical CNT/hollow Co3O4 microtubes. The proposed multi-step routes consist of multiple steps, i.e. the surface growth of ZIF-67 nanocrystals on PAN/Co(Ac)2 nanofibres in an ethanolic Hmim solution, dissolution of the PAN/Co(Ac)2 core in DMF, thermal annealing in an Ar (95%)/H2(5%) atmosphere at 750 °C, and calcination in air at 360 °C. Two-step reduction–oxidation treatments are important for the generation of unique hierarchical structures. Ar/H2 treatment completely reduced the CoOx species to Co metals which led to the formation of multiwalled CNTs throughout the microtubes. The subsequent mild oxidation treatment converted Co solid nanoparticles into Co3O4 hollow nanoparticles with size of 15–30 nm due to the Kirkendall effect. The resulting hierarchical microtubes were composed of hollow CNT/Co3O4 nanoparticles with inner diameters of 3.2/10.5 nm, respectively.

In a similar manner, PAN/Zn(Ac)2/Co(Ac)2@bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIF nanofibres were also prepared to obtain N-doped 1D carbon hollow tubules with tunable porosity and a large fraction of graphitic layers.425 The PAN/Zn(Ac)2/Co(Ac)2 core turned into carbon/ZnO/ZnxCo, which was then etched away via carbothermal reduction and evaporation. In situ TEM study during carbonization confirmed that the formation of ZnO – derived from Zn(Ac)2 – was only localized in the fibre core. This preparation process enabled the selective etching of the carbon core, which led to numerous internal voids. Therefore, the final porous/hollow structures were largely influenced by the amount of Zn(Ac)2 in the initial precursor solution. Indeed, the morphology changes from solid fibres to hollow tubules even with a few carbon nanosheet-shells were clearly observed with increasing amount of Zn(Ac)2.


Two-dimensional carbons. A 2D porous ZnO nanosheet was the first choice to demonstrate the preparation of 2D ZIF-8 and carbon materials with hierarchical porosity.427 On the other hand, layered double-metal hydroxide (LDH) and metal hydroxide have great potential as 2D scaffolds and self-sacrificial metal ion sources for in situ MOF growth as well. LDHs inherently possess a plentiful amount of coordinatively unsaturated metal sites on their surface, providing specific anchoring sites for MOF deposition. Indeed, in situ ZIF-67 deposition to CoAl-LDH nanoplatelets was demonstrated by Wei and coworkers.428 In their contribution, however, surface Co ions in CoAl-LDH served as only preferential nucleation sites and an additional Co source was needed for controllable ZIF-67 growth.

The use of LDH as a true reactive external template was demonstrated by Yamauchi and coworkers426 for preparation of 2D honeycomb-like carbon flakes. ZnCo-LDHs with a lateral size/thickness of 1 μm/10 nm were dispersed in a mixed solvent of DMF/H2O (9:1 v/v ratio), and subsequently transformed into ZnCo-ZIFs through the reaction with Hmim at 50 °C. ZIF polyhedra with a diameter of 100 nm began to grow on the flat surface of ZnCo-LDH after 4 h, and they formed densely packed flake-like networks after 8 h (Fig. 28a–d). The Zn/Co molar ratio in parent ZnCo-LDH was comparable to that in daughter ZnCo-ZIF, suggesting the homogeneous reaction between dissolved metal ions and Hmim. 2D-interconnected carbon flakes with abundant honeycomb-like macropores were produced via carbonization at 800 °C and acid etching, during which ZnCo-ZIF suffered from inhomogeneous framework shrinkage (Fig. 28e–g). This shrinkage is closely related to tightly packed networks of ZIF polyhedra. The connected edges that intimately adhere with adjacent polyhedra were stable and well-retained during carbonization. In contrast, the exposed single edges without intimate contact with the others were unstable and shrank toward the edge sides, thereby leading to the formation of honeycomb-like macropores. This formation strategy was also applicable to ZnCo-LDH with a different molar ratio and other layered metal hydroxides (Zn5(OH)8(NO3)2, Co(OH)2), allowing the preparation of carbon flakes with similar morphologies and honeycomb structures but with different specific surface area (226–860 m2 g−1), pore volume (0.75–1.45 cm3 g−1), nitrogen content (1.1–12.9 at%), and degree of graphitization.


Fig. 28 2D ZnCo-LDH derived synthesis of 2D ZIF and carbon flakes. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of ZIF networks. SEM images of (b) ZnCo-LDH-1, (c) ZnCo-ZIF-1-4 h, and (d) ZnCo-ZIF-1. (e) Schematic illustration of the pore formation mechanism. SEM images of (f and g) 2D honeycomb-like carbon flakes (Reproduced with permission.426 Copyright 2018, John Wiley & Sons).

Three-dimensional and hierarchical carbon. The methods to process MOs into complex morphologies and architectures are better established than those of MOFs, and thus could enrich the structure engineering tools for MOF-derived carbonaceous materials. Along this line, Song et al.429 employed pre-shaped ZnO templates with different morphologies (i.e. nanowires (1D), nanoplates (2D), and nanoflowers (3D)) as architecture directing agents for general dimension-controlled synthesis of hollow carbons. A versatile approach to synthesize core–shell ZnO@ZIF-8@Zn/Co (or Fe)-ZIF was presented. ZnO@ZIF-8 was easily prepared by adding Hmim to a ZnO suspension. However, in the case of bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIF shell growth, the sequence of precursor addition plays a crucial role in determining the final crystal morphologies. The simultaneous addition of Co salt and Hmim to the ZnO suspension led to the rapid formation of a Co(Hmim)42+ complex in the bulk solution (pathway 1 in Fig. 29a). The released Zn2+ directly reacted with the Co(Hmim)42+ and Hmim to form CoxZn1−x(Hmim)42+, rather than a Zn(Hmim)42+ complex. As a result, Zn/Co-ZIF nucleated either in bulk solution or on the rugged surface of ZnO. With increasing reaction time, Zn/Co-ZIF grew to 0D polyhedra by continuously extracting the Zn2+ ions from ZnO until its full dissolution. Because of this inhomogeneous and uneven growth process, part of the ZnO was encapsulated by the Zn/Co-ZIF layers, resulting in the formation of ZnO@Zn/Co-ZIF polyhedra (Fig. 29b). In contrast, one-by-one addition of Hmim followed by Co salt led to controlled epitaxial growth of ZIFs on ZnO templates (pathway 2 in Fig. 29a). ZIF-8 seed layers of ∼40 nm were rapidly formed on the surface of ZnO within a short time (<10 min). Subsequent addition of Co salt induced the formation of CoxZn1−x(Hmim)42+ specifically on the ZIF-8 layers, and therefore Zn/Co-ZIF uniformly nucleated and deposited onto the ZnO. The secondary Zn/Co-ZIF layers prohibited the complete dissolution of inner ZnO templates, forming the ZnO@ZIF-8@Zn/Co-ZIF heterostructures (Fig. 29c). The resulting ZnO@ZIF hybrids were transformed into hollow carbon/metal composites via one-step pyrolysis at 900 °C under a N2 atmosphere. ZnO was carbothermally reduced and evaporated upon pyrolysis, leaving hollow inner structures and macropores. Co and Fe metals catalyzed the formation of graphitic carbon and remained well-dispersed in the form of nanoparticles (Co) or single atoms (Fe). The particle morphologies inherited from parent ZnO templates (1D nanowires, 2D nanoplates, 3D nanoflowers) were well-preserved without significant deformation (Fig. 29d–f).
Fig. 29 Dimension controlled ZnO directed synthesis of hollow carbons. (a) Schematic representation of the growth of bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIF using ZnONW as a template with different precursor addition sequences. TEM images of (b) ZnO@Zn/Co-ZIF nanopolyhedron and (c) core–shell ZnONW@ZIF-8@Zn/Co-ZIF. SEM images and the corresponding TEM images (inset) of (d) 1D ZnONW@ZIF-8@Zn/Co-ZIF, (e) 2D ZnONP@ZIF-8@Zn/Co-ZIF, and (f) 3D ZnONF@ZIF-8@Zn/Co-ZIF. The products were collected at a reaction temperature of 45 °C after 2 h of reaction (Reprinted with permission.429 Copyright 2018, John Wiley & Sons).

Urchin-like nanorods of cobalt carbonate hydroxide (UNR CCH) were also utilized as architecture directing agents to prepare urchin-like nanotubes of Co single atoms/nitrogen doped carbon (UNT Co SAs/N-C).430 UNT ZIF-67 superstructures were firstly prepared by a hydrothermal reaction of UNR CCH powders with Hmim in a Teflon-lined autoclave at 150 °C, followed by HCl washing. They were subjected to thermal treatment at 500 °C in a sealed vacuum environment, which can facilitate the formation of well-dispersed Co single atoms. Therefore, multi-dimensional hierarchical tubes composed of carbon dodecahedron with embedded Co SAc were successfully synthesized.

The MO and hydroxide arrays with different morphologies and compositions can be grown on a wide variety of substrates (e.g. carbon cloth, metal foam/mesh/foil, and fluorine doped tin oxide (FTO) glass) by means of a hydrothermal reaction and electrochemical deposition.408 They are readily described in a number of literature examples and therefore can be used as versatile self-sacrificing architecture directing agents to realize 3D carbon arrays with complex morphologies and unique multiple functions.281,310,431–434 For instance, 3D architectures ensure the facile mass transport ability and the easy accessibility to catalytic active sites, and also support the MOFs to maintain their structural integrities without significant particle aggregation/collapse during thermal transformation. The substrates enhance the mechanical stability, and serve as a consecutive conductive network for fast electron/ion transport. As a result, such 3D integrated carbon composites have been popularly adopted as binder-free, conductive additive-free self-supported electrodes in energy storage and conversion devices, such as flexible supercapacitors, electrochemical water splitting, Zn–air batteries, and Li and Na ion batteries.281,310,408,431–434

In 2015, Duan and coworkers281 described the integration of MO arrays and a conductive substrate for the first time to develop ZnO@ZnO quantum dots (QD)/C core–shell nanorod arrays (NRA) on flexible carbon cloth (Fig. 30a–c). ZnO NRAs with diameter/length of 300 nm/4.5–7 μm were vertically grown on carbon cloth by a hydrothermal deposition route, and further served as a sacrificial template and Zn ion source for ZIF-8 shell growth. They reacted with the Hmim ligand in a mixed solvent of DMF/H2O (3:1 v/v ratio) at 70 °C, yielding ZnO@ZIF-8 NRAs. Thermal annealing under N2 gas flow at 650 °C led to the formation of ZnO@ZnO QD/C with multi-dimensional hierarchical architectures. Starting from identical ZnO@ZIF-8 NRA on carbon cloth, the same group also fabricated N-doped carbon bubbles supported on carbon tube arrays via catalytic deposition of ethanol and reduction–evaporation process.432 The ethanol vapor penetrated the ZIF-8 shell to access the surface of the ZnO core where the catalytic ethanol decomposition-carbon deposition occurred at high temperatures (500–800 °C). During this process the continuous ZIF-8 shell layer cracked into discrete ZIF-8 nanocrystals. The subsequent H2/Ar gas reduction at 800 °C rapidly generated nano-sized Zn metal, which was completely removed by evaporation without additional acid etching. This process directly converted the ZnO@C@ZIF-8 to hollow carbon tubes@N-doped carbon bubbles. Yang et al.433 further employed ZnO@ZIF-8 NRAs as versatile precursors for fabrication of other types of hybrid materials with tailored chemical structures and compositions. ZnO@C@CoNi-LDH arrays were prepared by pyrolysis of ZnO@ZIF-8 and subsequent electrochemical deposition of CoNi-LDH nanoplatelets on the external surface of ZnO@C. In parallel, Fe2O3@C NRAs were synthesized by converting ZnO@ZIF-8 to Fe(OH)3@ZIF-Fe through Zn-Fe cationic exchange in aqueous solution at room temperature, followed by pyrolysis in a N2 atmosphere at 450 °C. The resulting ZnO@C@CoNi-LDH and Fe2O3@C were used as self-supported electrodes for fabrication of asymmetric flexible all-solid-state supercapacitors.


Fig. 30 3D carbon array structures. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis procedures of ZnO@ZnO QDs/C core–shell NRAs on carbon cloth. (b and c) High and low magnifications of core–shell ZnO@ZnO QDs/C NRAs grown on flexible carbon cloth (Reproduced with permission.281 Copyright 2015, John Wiley & Sons). (d) Schematic illustration showing the fabrication of MO@MOF hybrid arrays and their derived MO@MOFC arrays on the 3D conductive matrix (Reprinted with permission.310 Copyright 2017, Elsevier). Electrosynthesis of ZIF-67 on Ni-foam supported (e and f) 1D ZnO nanorod, (g and h) 2D LDH nanowall, and (i and j) CoNi-alloy (Reproduced with permission.431).

Solid cobalt materials (cobalt carbonate hydroxide (CCH) and cobalt oxide) were integrated with conductive metal substrates to prepare similar 3D architectures having different chemical compositions. ZIF-67 was directly grown on well-aligned CCH nanorods in a Hmim/H2O/triethylamine solution. Subsequent treatments of oxidation, sulfurization, carbonization and phosphorization gave rise to hierarchically structured Co3O4@X composites (where X = Co3O4, CoS, C, and CoP).336 Huang et al.435 modified this procedure to prepare a CoP nanoarray wrapped with N, P-doped carbon on Ti foil. Instead of CCH arrays, Wang et al.436 used hierarchical CoO nanowires on Ni foam to synthesize CoO@ZIF-67 and in turn CoO@N-doped carbons.

Jiang and coworkers310 demonstrated fabrication of a series of well-defined MOF arrays/carbons grown onto various substrates (Fig. 30d). Different kinds of solid metal arrays (CoO, NiO and Cu(OH2) were directly grown on diverse substrates (Ni foam, Cu mesh, Cu foil, and Fe mesh) by a hydrothermal method, which then reacted with related organic ligands to form ZIF-67, Co-MOF-74 and HKUST-1. Pyrolysis under N2 gas flow at 500 °C yielded the corresponding MO@carbon on substrates with well-retained 3D array architectures. The authors found that the choice of appropriate solvents and solid metal precursors is critical for controlled MOF growth. For instance, the dissolution of CoO in ethanol is too slow to provide enough Co2+ ions for ZIF-67 formation, while that in water is too fast to confine the ZIF-67 nucleation on CoO nanorods exclusively. Furthermore, the use of Co3O4 instead of CoO only led to poorly defined heterostructures, possibly because of its limited dissolution under the given synthesis conditions. The successful preparation of CoO@ZIF-67 was achieved by using a mixed solvent of ethanol and water (1:1 v/v ratio). Therefore, it was revealed that the balance between the MO dissolution and MOF precipitation rate is crucial for the preparation of desired hybrid MOF/carbon arrays, which is also important for applications in the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) (refer to Section 6).

Despite the progress made, a versatile approach for continuous MOF deposition with controllable thickness and dimension is still considerably lacking. One possible strategy to overcome this challenge is electrodeposition of MOFs on shape-controlled MO/conductive substrates. Shao and coworkers431 demonstrated electrosynthesis of ZIF films on commercial conductive substrates (FTO glass, carbon fibre cloth, and Ni foam) (Fig. 30e–j). ZIF-8, ZIF-67 and bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIFs with various molar ratios and thicknesses were electrochemically deposited on conductive substrates (working electrodes). OH− generated from H2O on the working electrodes rapidly reacted with a M(Hmim)42+ complex to form M(mim)2 solids within tens of seconds at ambient temperature. The authors also reported electrodeposition of ZIFs to the nanostructured solid metal arrays with different morphologies (1D ZnO nanorod array, 2D CoAl-LDH nanowall array and 3D CoNi-alloy nanoframework), and their thermal transformation into hierarchically structured CNTs. In addition, electrodeposition could be highly attractive for the case in which direct growth of MOFs is not possible because of the incompatibility between the conductive substrate and MOFs. For instance, Feng et al.437 demonstrated electrodeposition of Co(OH)2 on a carbon fibre paper to deposit ZIF-67 in a controlled manner, which is not achievable by a direct MOF growth method. Very recently, Chen et al.438 vertically grew 2D Co-LDH nanosheets on HNO3-treated carbon cloth via a fast electrodeposition process, and utilized them as self-sacrificial templates that underwent a coordination reaction with Hmim in H2O/MeOH (1:1.5 v/v ratio) to induce ZIF-67 crystal growth and associated structural reconstruction. ZIF-67 dodecahedra tended to grow/graft on top of the nanosheets, resulting in the formation of 2D ZIF-67 nanosheets grafted with 3D ZIF-67 dodecahedra (2D/3D ZIF-67). They were transformed into ultrafine Co nanoparticle embedded 2D N-doped porous carbon nanosheets and 3D hollow N-doped porous carbon polyhedra, respectively.

Solution phase MOF deposition often involves the precise choice of solvents, solid metal precursors, substrates, ligands, and reaction temperature as well as potential risks for substrate corrosion and contamination. These factors can impose limitations on controlling the thickness and morphology of the MOF layer. To address this issue, solvent-free MOF growth techniques such as hot pressing,439 steam assisted conversion,440 and MOF-CVD271 have been developed in recent years. MOF-CVD has shown promise as a versatile method that conformally transforms complex 3D structures into continuous MOF films with uniform and controlled thickness by simply exposing the substrate to ligand vapor at a moderate temperature (e.g. 100 °C). Inspired by this, Young et al.434 reported MOF-CVD mediated synthesis of MOF arrays on carbon cloth. Hydrothermally grown ZnO and Co(CO3)0.5(OH)·0.11H2O arrays were exposed to Hmim vapor in a Teflon-lined autoclave at various temperatures (90–120 °C) and for various times (3–24 h) to deposit ZIF layers with controlled thickness. Two-step carbonization and oxidation treatments led to their conversion to MO/carbon composites (e.g. Co3O4/N doped carbon arrays on carbon cloth).


Challenges and opportunities. The reactive external template methods usually rely on multi-step procedures and thus are rather complicated and costly. However, the associated disadvantages could be largely compensated for by development of multi-functional, high-value added carbon materials that are otherwise not accessible. The fast and economical routes for fabrication of sophisticated architectures would be highly appreciated in the future. Solid precursors from large-scale industrial production or earth-abundant natural minerals are attractive low-cost raw materials. In addition, to date, the chemical composition of parent MOs is restricted to a few substances, mostly Zn and Co based ones. Many others still remain unexplored because of the lack of general criteria that govern the solid-to-MOF transformation. In the case of solution phase syntheses, the balance between the release rate of metal ions from the solid matter and the coordination rate of metal ions with ligands is critical for the controlled MOF growth. The solvent composition and reaction temperature also need to be optimized to localize the MOF nucleation only at the surface of MOs. However, an understanding of the underlying growth kinetics and thermodynamics is still considerably lacking. In this regard, high throughput methods are needed to rapidly screen the potential solid precursors and to establish the suitable transformation conditions. Taken together, there are still great opportunities to develop multi-functional carbonaceous materials constructed from hierarchically assembled multi-component building blocks (MO, MOFs, carbon, and substrates) through the reactive external template methods.
5.2.4. Carbon derived from top-down processed MOF. The aforementioned three strategies control the bottom-up assembly of metal ions and organic ligands in combination with various templates and substrates. In contrast, top-down processing uses the ‘pre-synthesized MOFs’ as building blocks for microfabrication (e.g. electrospinning,441 3D printing442), chemical etching443 and exfoliation444 to obtain desired morphologies. Thanks to the recent progress in the development of new processing technologies and the nano-MOFs, top-down MOF fabrication has become more easily accessible, thereby boosting the development of MOF-derived carbonaceous materials with unique structures and morphologies.
Zero-dimensional carbon. Chemical etching is a typical top-down approach to generate voids inside of solids. In recent years, controlled etching of preformed MOF crystals has attracted great interest as a means to prepare meso/macroporous MOF, hollow MOF, and complex multi-shelled MOF,367 opening up new opportunities for preparation of void-engineered carbon materials. Zhang et al.443 reported MOF nanobubbles with monocrystalline frameworks and their transformation to hollow carbons via pyrolysis (Fig. 31a). By simply immersing the preformed ZIF-8 in tannic acid solution for 5 min, hollow ZIF-8 nanobubbles with a thin shell of 15 nm were obtained. Tannic acid acts as both an etching agent and a surface protecting layer. It releases free H+ ions which can destroy the framework. Tannic acid itself is too large to penetrate into the MOF and thus adsorbs on the exposed surface of only MOFs, protecting the outer part from complete etching. In this way, the free H+ ions selectively etch the inner core of MOFs, generating hollow ZIF-8 nanobubbles. A particular advantage of the etching strategy is its ability to maintain inherent single-crystalline nature of parent MOFs, ensuring a highly ordered crystallographic orientation. Although other hollow MOFs have been prepared by direct growth/assembly of MOFs on spherical templates, they are composed of a number of aggregated MOF nanocrystals (i.e. polycrystalline) with ill-defined particle orientation and non-uniform size. In contrast, the etching-derived ZIF-8 nanobubbles retain monocrystalline nature as well as uniform and smooth surface which cannot be obtained from the conventional template methods. Moon and coworkers445 synthesized adamantane shaped, hierarchically micro- and macro-porous, N-doped carbon nanoframes via acid etching of ZIF-8 in an aqueous xylenol orange tetrasodium salt/HCl solution. Preferential etching along the 〈211〉 direction led to the formation of unique adamantane-like particle shape.
Fig. 31 Carbon derived from top-down processing of MOFs. (a) Acid etching derived synthesis of 0D carbon nanobubbles (licensed under CC-BY443). (b) Electrospinning of 1D MOF-based nanofibres from the precursor solution of preformed MOF nanoparticles and polymer fibres (Reproduced with permission.441 Copyright 2018, The Royal Society of Chemistry). (c and d) Electrospun ZIF-8/PAN-derived 1D porous carbon fibres (Reproduced with permission.446 Copyright 2017, The Royal Society of Chemistry). (e) Electrospun MIL-88@PAN-derived carbon necklace papers (Reproduced with permission.447 Copyright 2018, John Wiley & Sons). (f and g) Thermal exfoliation/carbonization of layered bulk metal-hexamine frameworks to 2D carbon materials (Reprinted with permission.448 Copyright 2018, The Royal Chemical Society). (h) The 3D printing diagram, and (i–k) 3D printed nitrogen doped mesoporous carbon flakes with embedded Co nanoparticles (Reproduced with permission.442 Copyright 2019, John Wiley & Sons).

One-dimensional carbon. Electrospinning is a simple and straightforward top-down approach for production of 1D nanofibres, and is by far the most extensively used method for processing MOFs/MDCs into 1D nanowires and nanotubes (Fig. 31b).441 In addition, the nanofibres can be directly deposited onto certain substrates or processed into functional textiles and free-standing papers. Combined with a large variety of nano-MOFs, electrospinning has great potential in the development of a number of new 1D porous carbon and carbon-based composite materials. Typical fabrication steps involve: (i) preparation of the precursor solutions of preformed nano-MOFs and polymers, (ii) electrospinning to MOF-based nanofibres, and (iii) carbonization. The intimate interaction between MOFs and polymeric binders is necessary to maintain the structural integrity of 1D nanofibres with uniformly distributed/confined MOF nanocrystals. For this purpose, polymers with functional moieties that can interact/coordinate with the exposed metal ions on the surface of MOFs have been widely used (e.g. PVP,449 PAN,323,446 poly(vinyl alcohol) (PVA)450). For instance, the surface Zn2+ ions in ZIF-8 can coordinate with the CN moieties present abundantly in PAN,446 which enables homogeneous and uniform distributions of ZIF-8 nanocrystals in the PAN matrix. Upon pyrolysis, PAN-derived carbons were generated at the early stage of thermal transformation, tightly encapsulating ZIF-8 nanocrystals to change their pyrolysis behavior. Because of the strong cohesive interfacial interaction between ZIF-8 and PAN-derived carbon, ZIF-8 decomposed with significant shrinkage from inside to outside, thereby generating uniform mesopores or hollow structures (Fig. 31c and d). In addition, several functional 1D porous carbon materials have been prepared from ZIF-8, ZIF-67 and bimetallic Zn/Co-ZIF.446,449–451 Interestingly, MOF (ZIF-67 and MIL-88) nanoparticles strung on the thin PAN fibres can be fabricated by carefully controlling the electrospinning conditions (Fig. 31e).447 The PAN strings bridged the MOF joints and entangled well to form an interconnected network, which led to the formation of free standing carbon necklace papers after pyrolysis under a N2 atmosphere.
Two-dimensional carbon. Top-down synthesis of MOF nanosheets relies on the exfoliation of layer-structured bulk MOFs.444 The weak interlayer interactions, e.g. van der Waals forces, hydrogen bonding, π–π stacking, can be broken by sonication and mechanical and chemical exfoliation. Although the exfoliated MOF nanosheets have shown promise in a myriad of applications,444 they are rarely exploited for the fabrication of 2D nanocarbons mainly because of the high cost and low yield of exfoliation methods, as well as structural collapse/restacking of nanosheets during carbonization. Recently, facile thermal exfoliations of layered MOFs into 2D N-doped carbon nanosheets448 and graphene stacks452 were reported. Fast pyrolysis (ramping rate >5 °C min−1) rapidly generated a large amount of gases which exfoliated/converted the layered bulk metal-hexamine frameworks to 2D carbon materials (Fig. 31f and g).448 Zhang et al.452 carbonized MOF crystals with stacked 2D lamellar structures at 500 and 900 °C for 1 h with a slow ramping rate (1 °C min−1). During this mild thermal treatment, gradual structural rearrangements occurred from MOF 2D subunits to 2D nanocarbon stacks. Xia et al.324 prepared N-doped porous graphene by thermal exfoliation of ZIF-L in the presence of LiCl/KCl salts under an inert atmosphere at 700–900 °C. The LiCl/KCl salts acted as an exfoliator and etching agent. The metal ions from molten salt (>369 °C) inserted into the crystal layers first, and then induced thermal exfoliation and formation of micro- and mesopores via vaporization at higher temperatures (>700 °C). Final 2D ultrathin carbon nanosheets were obtained after removal of residual metal salts and impurities through acid washing. Alternatively, Xu and coworkers376 combined the conformal transformation of MOF with chemical exfoliation to obtain few-layered graphene 2D nanoribbons. The MOF-derived solid carbon nanorods were exfoliated to two- to six-layered graphene nanoribbons through a KOH-assisted sonochemical treatment and thermal activation at 800 °C. This novel strategy enabled the preparation of high-quality graphene nanoribbons with high yield (>75%) and purity (>90%), which are higher than those obtained from the exfoliation of CNTs.
Three-dimensional carbon. 3D printing, also known as additive manufacturing, has attracted significant attention both in industry and academia.453 It allows direct fabrication of prototype-objects and patterns based on the digitally controlled deposition of successive layers of a material. Recently, this technology was successfully applied for manufacturing 3D complex structures of MOF-embedded polymers454 and MOF-derived hierarchically porous carbon frameworks.442 The printable ink was firstly prepared by mixing the Co-MOF (leaf-shape ZIF polymorph) and Pluronic F127 triblock copolymer in water, and then printed as fine filament layers to form a 3D Co-MOF framework (Fig. 31h).442 The 3D-printed mesh architectures enabled a uniform shrinkage to retain good mechanical stability without cracking during carbonization at 800 °C. The resulting 3D carbon frameworks have a hierarchical pore system (200 μm pores from inter-weaved filaments, meso- and micropores from Co-MOF-derived carbon flakes), uniformly embedded Co nanoparticles, high electrical conductivity and mechanical stability (Fig. 31h–k).
Challenges and opportunities. Top-down processing opens up new possibilities toward designable MDC architectures. Chemical etching enables the preparation of single-crystalline MDCs with tailored pore structures, yet it is highly dependent on the stability of MOFs against the etchant solution. Exfoliations are mostly applicable to a few layer-structured MOFs. The construction of high-quality 2D MOFs and their conformal transformation without aggregation, restacking and structural collapse still remain great challenges. Therefore, development of low-cost, high yield, and versatile top-down methods is highly desired. Integrations of MOFs with microfabrication technologies are attractive approaches to obtain MDCs with sophisticated morphologies. For instance, the use of emerging technologies, e.g. biomimetic 4D printing,455 may allow programmed fabrication of bio-inspired complex 3D architectures that are pre-encoded with unique functions.

6. Applications

6.1. General aspects

The control of material morphology as well as understanding processes associating with material application is extremely important due to the inherent connection between physical and chemical properties. The whole purpose of demonstrating noteworthy affinity between seemingly different materials (MO, MOF and porous carbon) ultimately has to convey to their utility. We must accentuate that besides chemical designability (e.g. chemical composition) of materials, physical designability (e.g. morphology) of materials is another essential factor. Physical designability of porous materials gives the opportunity to improve the material performance via design of the physical form rather than altering the chemical component, for instance, chitin assemblies build butterfly wings with nanostructures to impart hydrophobicity for a fly in rain and at the same time glaring colours to discourage predators. It is baffling to create a general scheme that directly links material's specific shape and size with its physical properties as both are mutually connected with the chemical composition. However, it is doable to show some pros and cons in certain applicability cases. For instance, it has been suggested that the size of nanomaterial particles has a strong influence on their toxicity, more specifically smaller particles might be more toxic per mass-based concentration.456 It led straight away to biomedical applications whereas surrounding particles such as cells and tissues or topical use can determine what nanomaterial size is required.457 The size of particles is also significant in catalysis, where along with the lowering of the size, the number of active sites increases, which is desired for catalyst efficiency.458 All in all, the application of porous materials is usually highly affected by the structuring and the morphology of the material, as both affects the optical properties, conductivities of electrons and ions, and the diffusion of gasses and liquids through the materials that have a significant impact on larger scale application for gas storage, membranes and their use for chemical transformations, be it as a catalyst itself or as a support.

6.2. MOF applications

MOF nanoparticles and their more complex structures found applications in many areas such as adsorption,225,459 separation,460 catalysis,461 sensing,462 optics,463–465 magnetics,466 drug delivery,467 bioimaging,468,469 tribology,470 patterning,241 as well as data-,471 energy-250 and gas472 storage. Generally, for each application homogeneity of nanocrystals is mainly required. Nevertheless, to obtain detailed information about the dependence of morphology and implementation, it is important to present and distinguish how e.g. length of rods or size of globules affects process parameters. Thus, here we report examples of different applications of MOF materials that are affected by both the shape and size of their particles.

MOFs are well known due to their porosity and high specific surface areas; thus, they are a natural choice of materials for adsorption and separation.473 Size and morphology are also valid for such processes, especially in terms of kinetics.474 Homogeneous and smaller particles are usually more preferred to minimize diffusion limitations,475 and along with that adsorption equilibrium time decreases.476 Non-spherical anisotropic crystals are highly required when it is necessary to obtain proper orientation in films or membranes.477 Peculiar shapes are often less stable, whereas for sorption procedures defect-free surfaces are mostly needed.280 Thicker films are usually more rigid, but they permeate much less gas or liquid. For that the synthesis of MOF-membranes with thickness less than 100 nm has to be considered.478

The shape of the MOF crystals could also have an impact on the application of membranes in adsorption and separation processes. Some of them have a tendency to agglomerate which leads to a decrease in membrane selectivity. Thus, a compromise must be involved between choosing sophisticated or durable membranes without defects, such as pinholes or cracks,479,480 which ends up in either better selectivity or permeability.481 An interesting example of how tuning MOF crystals morphology affected membrane applicability was presented by Zuo and Chung.482 Their goal was to obtain a nature-mimetic hydrophobic membrane for seawater desalination, by covering a pristine ceramic tube surface with NH2-MIL-53(Al). Depending on the source two different types of crystals were fabricated, flower-like and ordered nanocrystals (Fig. 32). The first type was characterized with a high aspect ratio which promoted effective membrane surface area, but the nanocrystals were poorly aligned. The second MOF morphology made of small crystals was more suited for the set goal since nanocrystals were small enough to cover the whole surface leading to lower defect probability. The influence of the MOF crystal morphology is particularly noticeable in MMM (mixed-matrix membrane) composites, consisting of MOF particles in a polymer matrix. Rodenas et al.483 introduced a copper-based MOF (CuBDC) with different morphologies like nanosheets and bulk crystals into the matrix. The prepared composites exhibited very different efficiency in CO2/CH4 separation. More uniform dispersion of nanosheets in the form of lamellae-like structures showed higher separation factors.


Fig. 32 Flower-like (a) and ordered nanocrystals (b) morphology of NH2-MIL-53(Al) of the second growth tubes for seawater desalination (licensed under CC-BY482).

MOF storage capabilities are strictly the result of well-developed specific surface area and morphology ensuing from the crystalline facets. Gases, drug particles, pollutants or electrolytes differ in particle size. Due to the wide range of MOF crystal shapes, it is possible to control pore accessibility and dictate the way how such particles will be adsorbed, dispersed, bonded and stabilized. In the research of Li et al.484 it was presented how sulfur performance in Li–S batteries was improved by using ZIF-8 with different sizes. In the range between 20 nm and 1 μm, the smallest MOF particles were the most efficient with sulfur implementation; however, the best cycling stabilization was achieved with average ones (∼200 nm). Most beneficial dimensions of the particles were called ‘golden size’, which definitely are difficult to discover and match with proper utility; nevertheless, it should be fundamental for every conducted research. In another study, it was suggested that defined morphology made of MOF crystals can improve gas storage. Zr- and Cr-MOFs within electrospun nanofibres revealed higher loadings towards H2 and N2 than single crystals.485

Bioapplication of MOFs is mainly focused not only on drug delivery, but also on cancer cell targeting.486 For the sake of biocompatibility, non-toxicity and safe decomposition of the selected OBUs and IBUs, they must be precisely investigated towards metabolic processes and reactivity of other biocompounds. Besides the composition, undoubtedly the morphology of the drug carrier has to be considered. It can enhance the dispersion of the API (active pharmaceutical ingredient), stabilize the API-MOF system and also have an impact on materials motion in human body fluids. The comparison of loading anti-cancer drug oridonin into nano- and micro-sized MOF-5 revealed higher capacities and later better drug release from the smaller particles.487 In the research presented by Lin et al.488 nanoplates of DBP-UiO (Hf-porphyrin based MOF) exhibited exceptional behavior in photodynamic therapy for head and neck cancer. Results suggested that specific MOF morphology and porosity was helpful in reactive oxygen species generation, and further cancerous tissue atrophy.

Atomically dispersed active sites are the major attribute of MOFs for their exquisite catalytic performance including heterogeneous catalysis,489,490 photo- and electrocatalysis (i.e. water splitting,491,492 CO2 reduction,493,494 organic transformations,495 and oxygen reduction reactions496). To increase the catalytic activity of materials enhancing their surface area is the key and can be executed in two ways: synthesizing either very small nanoparticles or super-thin films.497 Also changing the shape of the MOF shell in the core–shell structures via modulating kinetic factors (nucleation/dissolution) enables unusual size selectivity of the products in many catalytic processes.498 When it comes to applying 2D materials such as nanosheets in catalysis, features like rigidity, porosity and smoothness are highly dependent on their thickness. Jia et al.499 presented bivalent CoFe-MOFs with a thickness of 1.3 nm. By adjusting the membrane or film thickness it became possible to create suitable catalysts for specific purposes. It is surprising that thicker films containing mesopores in the structure are less catalytically active than non-porous thin films. Ultrathin films made of only a few atomic layers have easily accessible active sites, which is crucial in electrocatalysis. However, such thin sheets are more potent to damage or disperse in various solvents. Additionally, ultrathin films lack roughness, thus they are much smoother than thick membranes and often the development of the porous network in their structure is not observed. Han et al.500 presented the influence of Zn-MOFs [Zn3(BTC)2(H2O)3] morphology on electrochemical reduction of CO2 to CH4. The shape of materials achieved varied from rod-like to sheet-like and spherical. A sheet-like Zn-MOF had the most beneficial influence on current density, increasing it mostly due to its largest electroactive surface area. Therefore, it was the most efficient in the reduction process. Another morphology-dependent case of MOF application was the photodegradation of methylene blue by coordination polymer Prussian white (PW).501 The specific shape of the hollow cubic structures increased decomposition of the organic dye compared to cubic crystals, almost 3 times after 5 min and 2.5 time after 25 min (Fig. 33).


Fig. 33 (a) FESEM image of the sample prepared following a typical procedure. (b) TEM and (c, d) high magnification FESEM images of a single hierarchical PW crystal. Inset: Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern taken from the edge section. (Reprinted with permission.501 Copyright 2014, The Royal Chemical Society.)

For electrochemistry and sensing, MOF materials are perfect not only because of their porosity which allows ion storage and transport but also electrochemical stability and guest/host interactions.502 Once again low-dimensional crystallites favor increasing electrode surface area to shorten diffusion paths. For instance, Zhou et al.503 presented a 2D few-layer structure of NiCo-MOF and black phosphorus, which simplified charge transfer and increased battery efficiency. Depending on usage, rigid or flexible MOFs are highly required. Chemical and mechanical stability are important especially when it comes to operating devices where the dangerous short circuit may occur. Choosing short and rigid linkers with redox-inactive nodes reinforces the MOF material, and by obtaining the appropriate size of the nanoparticles all together it can amplify stability. It is particularly necessary for electrodes where dendrite formation happens. However, flexibility and malleability are handy in systems where materials’ volume expands and contracts. In this situation long and stretchy ligands that can form crystals with the smallest structural tensions are preferable.504

Formerly, bulky MOF materials limited their applicability in highly sensitive sensors. Now, when it is feasible to obtain single nanocrystals it became possible to use them in detecting very small molecules.505 Morsali et al.506 compared bulk sample and plate-like nanocrystals of [Zn(OBA)(H2DPT)0.5]·DMF (TMU-34) for Al(III) sensing. The nanomaterial exhibited not only a faster response but also a higher detection limit. The plurality of ligands and cations allows creating MOF crystals with the required composition and morphology that will be able to trap a nanoparticle with a simultaneous reaction in the form of a structural change. For example, rare bundle-like morphology of Eu based MOFs was used as a platform to detect styrene volatile vapors.507 Fluorescent MOFs are an interesting case, proving how sensitive these materials can be. It was suggested that the response to the stimulus may be changing the distance between nodes by shortening/lengthening the linkers, and as a result changing MOF fluorescence.508 Similarly to previous applications, the size of the nanoparticles matters in sensing. It was presented by Nenoff et al.509 for I2 detection by using diverse MFM-300(X) materials (X = Al, Fe, In or Sc with biphenyl-3,3′,5,5′-tetracarboxylate as linker). The amounts of adsorbed and then polarized I2 molecules were different depending on the quantum size and morphology of MFM-300, which ultimately led to a distinct electrochemical response.

6.3. MOF-derived carbon applications

As already mentioned in the preceding sections, MDCs are of significant interest for various applications, e.g. electrochemical energy storage, electrocatalysis, nanomedicine or sensing. In particular, the opportunity to tailor the obtained carbon material via the precursor morphology, i.e. in 0D, 1D, 2D and 3D, has a profound effect on materials properties and applicability. As such the approach going from solids to MOF to carbon is an effective way to introduce additional versatility in the finally obtained carbon structures.

In energy-related applications, high specific surface area is one of the required features of MDC electrode materials because it considerably influences charge and discharge rates, which are significant performance indicators in modern energy storage.316 Moreover, through tailoring the structure of MDC pores that mediate ion access to electrodes, exchange kinetics can be improved. For example, the ratio of micropores to mesopores is crucial to ameliorate electrochemical properties of MDCs321 or the accessibility of active sites.510 The morphology of MDCs also has a significant impact on the overall efficiency of energy-related devices as the material needs to sequester charge carrying species.510 Therefore, 2D nanostructures are of interest due to their high surface area and good compatibility with device integration.342 In addition, MDCs contain residual metal ions that can have a positive effect on performance but might also interfere with catalytic reactions at the surface.511

The well-developed surface area of materials is especially important in catalysis and sensing because it affects reaction rates and sensitivity.512 One of the possible approaches to maximize the number of active sites on solid catalysts is to lower the size of the their particles.513 Additionally, the morphology of materials has a significant influence on molecular transport at the surface in diffusion-controlled processes. As such, tuning the shape of the catalyst particles can remarkably alter the catalytic activity and selectivity due to the significant variations in the surface atomic configurations. Particularly for catalysis, spherical structures are advantageous because of short pathways for the diffusion of species, dispersion enhancement and easy attachment to supports. Moreover, catalyst nanoparticles with an anisotropic morphology can greatly mediate the reaction properties by selectively exposing desired crystal facets.514 The morphology can affect the mechanism of the metal–support interaction as well, which is one of the most fundamental issues in heterogeneous catalysis. In nanomedicine, drug loading and designed release show a significant relation to high surface area.515 Additionally, pore structures, sizes and connections can be utilized to modulate and design release characteristics.516 Spherical systems are of particular use as they minimize viscous effects and can be functionalized homogeneously. Moreover, for smaller particles with a higher surface-to-volume ratio, therapeutic amounts of drugs can be loaded on their surfaces.517 Nevertheless, shape-anisotropic nanoparticles have been found to exhibit specific interactions with biological cells depending on particle morphology.518 Furthermore, metal ions in the final product can be useful for photothermal therapy and diagnostic tasks, e.g. imaging and sensing.

Adsorption is one of the classical applications of MOFs and MDCs, which is mainly related to their porosity and surface chemistry.316,519 Surface chemistry can be easily tailored via MO/MOF compositions. It should be noted that especially nitrogen-doped MDCs are promising adsorbents for gases including H2 and CO2.316 The pore connectivity and sizes, as well as the morphology of carbon materials also have an impact on the rate of adsorption and selectivity.

Carbon materials are often used in the design of supercapacitors that are of particular interest in portable electronics.321,520 The capacitance and energy density of supercapacitors rely to a significant extent on the porosity and surface area of the utilized electrode material. Moreover, pore sizes and structures have a profound influence on supercapacitor activity, where the utilization of well-defined precursors, i.e. MOFs have a tremendous impact.521–523 In regard of future applications as devices, the development of appropriate supercapacitors is of utmost importance. In order to tackle that challenge, Shao and coworkers433 fabricated flexible carbon-based electrodes as mentioned in Section 5.2.3. To obtain a supercapacitor two nanorod arrays were generated and combined, namely ZnO@C@CoNi-LDH (as anode) and Fe2O3@C (as cathode). The nanorod array itself served as a template to obtain high surface areas including abundant and accessible active sites. After addition of PVA–KOH as a solid electrolyte, a flexible supercapacitor was designed. A high energy density of 1.078 mW h cm−3 was obtained with an outstanding cycling stability of 95% capacity retention over 10000 cycles. In a similar way, Wang and coworkers524 assembled a flexible supercapacitor from MOF-derived nanocarbon arrays on carbon cloth (Fig. 34a and b). Finally, active MnO2 or Bi2O3 was deposited on the carbon substrate. A 2D Cu-MOF-based approach was developed by Li et al.208 A 2D MOF was synthesized from Cu2+ and 1,4-bis(2-methyl-imidazol-1-yl)butane) as a ligand to afford stacked lamella. Subsequently, the material was calcined, etched with HCl and activated by KOH to obtain carbon nanosheets with ultrahigh porosity (BET surface area of 2491 m2 g−1). The defined layers with an interspace of the MOF precursor were translated into the final carbon material, which was reflected in an excellent specific capacitance (260.5 F g−1 at 0.5 A g−1) and energy density (18.38 W h kg−1). The correlation between morphology and capacitance was investigated by Schmidt and coworkers.363 Plate-like 2D MOFs and rod-like hexagonal MOFs were synthesized as described in Section 5.2.1 and carbonized to form the respective 2D or rod-like carbon structures. The performance of the material in supercapacitors was evaluated. Interestingly, a distinct difference in volumetric capacitance was observed, while the specific capacitance was almost the same for both carbon structures. This result shows that even the overall shape of the carbon has an influence on performance, which is a good starting point for future developments of more sophisticated architectures.


Fig. 34 (a) Fabrication process of the MDC-decorated electrodes: MnO2 as cathode and Bi2O3 as anode are directly grown on the CC/MDC surface and (b) two photos showing the size and flexibility of the electrodes (Reprinted with permission.524 Copyright 2018, John Wiley & Sons). (c and d) Schematic illustration of 1D nanocarbon synthesis from porous N-doped CNFs to CHTs with an enlarged graphene interlayer spacing and (e) rate capability and cycling performance (Reproduced with permission.425 Copyright 2017, Elsevier).

In addition to supercapacitors, batteries constitute another important type of device in electrochemical energy storage. Here, carbon materials are frequently utilized as electrode materials as well. Similar to supercapacitors, porosity, morphology and high surface area are the key parameters to obtain superior charge/discharge characteristics. Nevertheless, one of the major challenges is long-term stability and high performance at high current rates.522 In particular, Zn-based batteries are frequently investigated.525,526 Sun and coworkers527 fabricated a hierarchical electrode material based on MnO2 nanorods for Zn-batteries. At first MnO2 nanorods were synthesized via the hydrothermal method and coated with PVP as a support for MOF synthesis. Subsequently, ZIF-8 was formed on the surface of PVP and carbonized via thermal treatment in the final step that led to a partial reduction of MnO2 to a porous MnOx structure. The porous MnOx structure facilitated ion transport and acted as a buffer space to alleviate volume expansion/contraction during discharge/charge processes, which is a significant feature for long-term stability. Meanwhile, the MOF-derived N-doped carbon enabled efficient electron transport. For electrochemical performance, Zn was utilized as an anode and MnOx@N-C as a cathode as well as reference materials (MnO2 and MnOy). Due to the porous structure and conductive carbon network, superior electrochemical properties, e.g. cycling stability, high capacity and rate capability, were observed. In depth studies on the charge/discharge mechanism showed that the superior performance at high rates is due to the short length of ion diffusion and rapid electron transfer. In addition to Zn batteries, Li-ion batteries belong to the most important types of batteries.528,529 Thus, Duan and coworkers281 studied MOF-derived anodes for Li-ion batteries, as described in Section 5.2.3. The resulting ZnO@ZnO quantum dots/carbon core–shell nanorod arrays on flexible carbon cloth showed high specific capacities and rate capabilities, which was ascribed to the porous shell layer and single-crystal ZnO core. In the area of sodium batteries, Goodenough and coworkers425 employed nitrogen-doped carbon anodes formed from MOF precursors (Fig. 34c–e, Section 5.2.3). First, PAN was combined with zinc acetate/cobalt acetate and nanofibres were obtained via electrospinning (PAN/M(Ac)2 film). After introduction of Hmim, a ZIF network was formed on the nanofibre surface (PAN/M(Ac)2@ZIF film) and the whole composite material was carbonized to undergo a transition from carbon nanofibres (CNF) to carbon hollow tubules (CHT). Finally, the N-doped carbon material was utilized as an anode in a sodium battery with excellent rate capability, high capacity (up to 346 mA h g−1) and cycle performance (over 10000 cycles). The superior performance was attributed to the structural features. For example, the enlarged spacing of carbon layers facilitated effective Na+ storage, while the high surface area and porous structure enabled short diffusion distance for Na+ ions. Thus, sufficient contact between the active material and electrolyte was introduced for a fast charge-transfer process. Moreover, the long-aspect ratio of tubular structures improves 1D electron transport and electrical percolation. The presence of defects in MDC further assists Na+ diffusion from outer to inner carbon for a rapid activation of the whole carbon material.

In the realm of electrochemical energy conversion, fuels cells and proton exchange membrane fuel cells (PEMFCs) play a significant role. One of the main challenges in PEMFCs is the sluggish kinetics and high overpotential of the ORR as well as electrode durability.532 A solution to the problem, albeit an expensive one, is the utilization of Pt as a catalyst. One option to improve catalyst activity is to tailor the catalytic interfaces, e.g. the support material or the grain size of catalytic particles as well as their distributions.533 For example, increased surface area and conductivity of the carbon support can have a profound influence on catalyst activity.319 A bimetallic catalyst based on Pt/Ni was reported by Zhao and coworkers.534 MOF-5 derived carbons with well-developed surface area (1453–3174 m2 g−1) were used as support materials for subsequent deposition of Pt and Ni through modified polyol methods. At last the ORR activity was investigated, showing a significantly increased activity compared to commercial Pt/C, which might be of interest for PEMFC. Due to the significant cost of Pt, many researchers are looking into cheap, earth-abundant alternatives. Liao and coworkers535 fabricated a MDC electrode for PEMFC. Ferrocene was introduced into ZIF-8 via evaporation and carbonized to a porous N-doped carbon with embedded single Fe atoms. Subsequently, PEMFCs were assembled and the performance evaluated showing excellent results in the cathodic ORR in H2/O2 systems (current density of 1100 mA cm−2 at 0.6 V and power density of 775 mW cm−2), while decent performance was also observed in H2/O2 and H2/air systems. Although the catalyst durability was higher than the literature references, improvements are still needed to reach industrial standards. Very recently, Zhao and coworkers530 prepared an ORR catalyst via a multicomponent ball milling approach (Fig. 35a–c). PAN and ZIF-8 as carbon precursors were combined with NaCl and Fe-tris-1,10-phenanthroline (Fe-TPI) as a morphological modulator and a Fe-source, respectively. The precursor was pyrolyzed to obtain a carbon material consisting of microporous nanosheets, embedded macropores and dispersed Fe-N4 sites (SA-3DNC). The obtained catalysts showed exceptional activity in PEMFC at high current density and improved durability, which was attributed to the hierarchical pore structure of PAN-based carbon.


Fig. 35 (a) Preparation of SA-3DNC for ORR catalysis in acidic media, (b) Tafel plots of SA-3DNC and references (20% Pt/C, Z8-Fe-P, PAN-Fe-P) and (c) linear scan voltammetry plots of SA-3DNC and references (20% Pt/C, Z8-Fe-P, PAN-Fe-P) before and after the 10000-cycle durability test. (Reprinted with permission.530 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society). (d) Illustration of formation of porous N-doped carbon with single W atoms (W-SAC) and (e) Tafel plots of the W-SA (Reprinted with permission.531 Copyright 2019, John Wiley & Sons).

In contrast to the ORR that is utilized in fuel cells, electrochemical water splitting is an important process to generate hydrogen, i.e. the HER on the cathode and the oxygen evolution reaction (OER) on the anode.536,537 Here also MOF-derived carbon is utilized frequently to improve the electrocatalytic process due to conductivity from carbon and catalytic centres from metal ions.538,539 As mentioned in Section 5.2.3 Jiang and coworkers310 introduced a method to fabricate well-aligned MOF arrays and turned them into self-standing electrodes. The porous N-doped carbon arrays with incorporated Ni and CoO were utilized for the HER and OER. A superior performance compared to reference materials e.g. CoO@CoNC on Ni foam was observed. Moreover, the catalytic material showed only negligible deactivation in the OER and HER after 3000 cycles. The improved electrocatalytic performance was attributed to the porous nanoarray electrode architecture, in situ carbon incorporation, increased accessibility of active sites, accelerated release of gas bubbles, robust structure as well as improved mass and charge transport. Single W atoms on N-doped carbon were utilized by Wang and coworkers for the HER (Fig. 35d and e).531 A UiO-66-NH2-based MOF was formed from WCl5, 2-amino-benzenedicarboxylic acid and ZrCl4. After pyrolysis and HF etching, well-defined porous N-doped carbon with homogeneously dispersed W atoms (W-SAC) was obtained, which was subsequently used as an electrode in the HER. In the electrochemical investigation, a small overpotential was found with an overall activity similar to Pt/C. DFT calculations showed that the W1N1C3 unit played the most important role in HER performance, which opens up new opportunities for further improvement in the future.

MOFs are well-known for their catalytic activity,542–544 which also counts for MDCs. Jagadeesh and coworkers540 used a Co-MOF as a template to form an efficient carbon catalyst for hydrogenation of benzonitrile (Fig. 36a and b). A Co-BDC MOF was pyrolyzed to obtain an activated carbon catalyst with embedded Co3O4- and Co-nanoparticles. The catalyst did not require nitrogen doping and it could be utilized on a large gram scale and recycled in a versatile way. Qian and coworkers545 applied a Ni-based MOF to synthesize a carbon-based hydrogenation catalyst. A Ni-BTC MOF was pyrolyzed and used for the reduction of 4-nitrophenol with a significant catalytic efficiency. Finally, the catalysts could be recovered with a magnet, which allowed easy recycling. Zhao and coworkers546 combined graphitic carbon nitride (g-CN) with ZIF-8 derived carbon for enhanced photocatalysis. At first ZIF-8 particles were carbonized and deposited on the g-CN 2D surface. Next, visible light induced photocatalytic degradation of bisphenol A was studied. The improved catalytic activity was attributed to the enhanced light harvesting and efficient interfacial charge separation between g-C3N4 and ZIF-8-derived carbon. Such a catalyst is of significant interest for waste water remediation as it can be used to degrade small molecule pollutants, i.e. drug residues or residues from agriculture.


Fig. 36 (a) Preparation of graphene encapsulated cobalt oxide- and cobalt-nanoparticles supported on carbon by the pyrolysis of cobalt-terephthalic acid MOF-carbon template and (b) the recycling of cobalt-terephthalic acid MOF@C-800-catalyst for the hydrogenation of benzonitrile to benzylamine (reaction conditions: 5 mmol benzonitrile, 250 mg catalyst, (3.8 mol% Co), 20 mL toluene, 25 bar H2, 5 bar NH3, 120 °C, 16 h) (licensed under CC-BY540). (c) Schematic formation of Fe, La and Zr-MOFs, calcination to obtain MDC, formation of AChE-modified electrodes and their inhibition curves for methyl parathion (Reproduced with permission.541 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society).

One of the common applications of MOFs is sensing.470,547,548 Both enzymatic and non-enzymatic sensors are described in the literature. Moreover, carbon materials derived from MOFs are utilized in sensing as well, which can be ascribed to several factors.549–551 On the one hand, MDC presents a defined environment for analytes that might be useful for the selectivity of the sensor. On the other hand, the defined environment enables a precise deposition and implementation of the (bio)recognition site, e.g. a reactive group or enzyme. Finally, the high surface area of MOFs and MDCs allows increasing number and rate of interactions with the analyte, which leads to improved sensitivity. As such, improved structural control has a profound influence on sensor performance, i.e. sensitivity and selectivity, which also leads the direction for future improvements. Thus, the MDC approach allows specific tailoring of the environment for the respective sensing application, e.g. with respect to analyte concentration, presence of competing analytes and rate of analysis. Dong and coworkers541 described MDC nanocomposites for enzyme immobilization (Fig. 36c). Fe3+, Zr4+ and La3+ were combined with 2-aminoterephthalate to form three MOFs. After carbonization, acetylcholinesterase was immobilized in the carbon/MOF structures to perform biosensing of methyl parathion. The La3+-derived structures showed the best performance, which was attributed to their hierarchical and highly porous architecture. As such, increased amounts of the enzyme could be immobilized, electron transfer enhanced and diffusion length of the analyte on the electrode surface shortened. Therefore, biosensors with a linear detection range for concentrations from 1.0 × 10−13 to 5 × 10−19 g mL−1 and the lowest detection limit of 5.8 × 10−14 g mL−1 were fabricated. A non-enzymatic glucose sensor was described by Fransaer and coworkers.552 HKUST-1 was grown on a Cu-foam substrate and pyrolyzed to obtain carbon particles with embedded Cu nanoparticles on the Cu-substrate. The fabricated electrode showed high sensitivity, low detection limit and good stability for the non-enzymatic detection of glucose. The superior performance was benefitted from the hierarchical structure of the electrode, the synergistic effect of Cu and carbon as well as their close contact. A molecularly imprinted sensor was presented by Ma and coworkers.553 On a glassy carbon electrode, carbon nanotubes and MOF-5 derived carbon were deposited. After cleaning, pyrrole was electropolymerized on the carbon surface in the presence of cysteine to obtain molecular imprinting for the cysteine molecule. The sensors could be utilized for enantioselective detection of cysteine enantiomers and then regenerated via an overoxidation process and pH adjustment.

Nanomedicine is another important area of application for MOFs.554–556 Hence, MDCs found some application in nanomedicine as well with particular areas as controlled drug delivery, bio-imaging and photothermal therapy.515 The MDCs bring several features that are useful for nanomedicine. For example, improved drug loading and delivery due to well-developed surface area, tunable pore size and functionalizable surface. Metal incorporation enables therapeutic effects, e.g. photothermal energy conversion. In addition, the combination with fluorescent material facilitates diagnostic tasks, like imaging and sensing. MDCs provide promising supports to combine diagnostic and therapeutic tasks in one system. Liu and coworkers557 described the sonodynamic therapy via MDC. ZIF-8 was coated with mesoporous SiO2 to prevent aggregation, and pyrolyzed and etched to obtain mesoporous carbon nanoparticles. The key for sonodynamic therapy was the porphyrin-like metal centres formed during the pyrolysis process. Next, sonodynamic therapy was studied in vitro and in vivo. The particles were introduced into tumor tissues via the enhanced permeability and retention effect. Upon irradiation with ultrasound, reactive oxygen species were generated, e.g. singlet O2 as well as hydroxyl radicals that have the therapeutic effect to induce tumor cell apoptosis. Due to the high surface area and pore structure a strong cavitation effect in combination with ultrasound was observed that greatly enhanced the sonodynamic therapy efficiency. In the realm of drug delivery, Yamauchi and coworkers558 studied ZIF-8 derived carbon nanoparticles as intracellular carriers. A significant loading of cis-platin drug and a zero-order release profile in the first 20 h were observed. The carbon nanoparticles exhibited excellent biocompatibility even at doses above 100 μg mL−1 and intracellular uptake was observed in HepG2 cells. Fan et al.559 described MDC nanosheets for bacteria eradication and anti-infective therapy (Fig. 37). ZIF-8 was grown on GO sheets, carbonized subsequently to obtain Zn2+-doped carbon on graphene and finally, oxidation led to ZnO on graphene. Next, poly(N-isopropylacrylamide) was grafted on the surface to introduce a photothermal response and obtain the final therapeutic material (TRB-ZnO@G). Overall, the obtained materials entailed three modes to eradicate bacteria, i.e. Zn2+ release, mechanical cutting of the cell membrane due to the sheet like structure and photothermal hyperthermia via NIR irradiation. In the area of bioimaging, an example of MDC was described by Xu and coworkers.366 Carbon nanodots were formed via carbonization of ZIF-8 precursors. Fluorescent particles were obtained and used for cell imaging, which is an important application in biomedical diagnostics and cell biology.
